Supply Chains in the Maritime Industry 
with a Moving Destination Point 
DTU Management Engineering 
Department of Management Engineering 
==PAGE:2==
Abstract 
The hard competition in today’s global markets and the customers high expectations has made 
companies invest more in their supply chains, where most companies share the common goal of 
servicing their customer in the most efficient and effective way. Supply Chain Management is a tool 
employed by companies in order to deliver products and services in a cost effective and efficient way 
across the whole supply chain. 
The existing literature provides numerous studies in various industries regarding Supply Chain 
Management. However, the literature indicates a research gap due to limited amount of studies 
concerning SCM in the maritime industry specifically and the impact an end user of a product has on 
strategic decision making.  
Each supply chain has its end user. In the maritime industry the end users are vessels, which are moving 
units, and it is therefore assumed that it is more difficult for supply chains to cope with product delivery 
to this type of end users. This is considered an indication that the end user could affect the supply chain 
strategy decisions of companies in the maritime industry. Consequently, the project of this report is to 
investigate the applicable supply chain strategy for a case company in the maritime industry, i.e. for a 
product with a moving destination point, in order to address this research gap.  
The case study is based on the case company Maersk Oil Trading. The project entails a decision on how 
to choose the right supply chain strategy, how to structure the supply chain and align it with the chosen 
strategy, as well as an analysis of the end user to see if it affects the decision on the supply chain strategy. 
Henceforth, a framework is proposed together with a six step guideline. This framework and guidelines 
are considered for other companies with a moving destination point to use when deciding on a supply 
chain strategy for its product or service.

1. Introduction  
Supply Chain Management is a relatively new field and the topic has risen over the last 20 years 
(Mentzer, Myers, & Stank, 2007). Now it is difficult to pick up a magazine on manufacturing, 
distribution, marketing or transportation without seeing an article about supply chain management 
related topics.  
The definition of a supply chain is a set of relationships between suppliers, manufacturers, distributors 
and retailers that enables the transformation of raw materials into final products (Beamon, 1998). Even 
though a supply chain consists of many stages the chain ought to be considered as a single unit. Many 
researchers limit their analysis within an individual stage but as Beamon (1998) mentioned there is more 
need for an integrated approach for analysing the supply chain as a whole, i.e. its design and 
performance. Most companies today, which either sources globally, sells globally or competes with a 
company that does so, focuses on global supply chain management. Although the extraordinary growth 
of global supply chain management proves its robustness and practical importance, the field is diffuse 
and complex (Mentzer, Myers, & Stank, 2007). The supply chain is often a big and intricate network 
spread throughout the world, which makes it difficult to find the globally optimal integrated solution 
(Simchi-Levi, Kaminsky, & Simchi-Levi, 2000). A big supply chain network often has various facilities 
with variable conflicting objectives, e.g. suppliers want manufacturers to purchase large quantities in 
stable volumes with flexible delivery dates while the manufacturers needs to be flexible to their 
customers’ requirements and changing demand and therefore cannot implement long production runs.  
Simchi-Levi et al. (2000) stated that the supply chain is constantly changing and it is a dynamic system 
that evolves over time. According to Fisher (1997) the answer to coordination and collaboration 
problems within supply chain management is to make sure a company finds the right supply chain for 
each product. The root cause of the problems troubling many supply chains is a mismatch between the 
type of product and the type of supply chain. Therefore, it is important to figure out what is the right 
supply chain for the product in question (Fisher, 1997). 
1.1 Motivation 
When improving a supply chain the product travelling through it should be considered (Hernán, 2013). 
According to Hernán (2013) it is difficult to expect a supply chain to be efficient, fast, agile, custom-
configured and flexible all at once. These set of skills are incompatible within the same supply chain.  
Contemporary technology enables good coordination between different stages of the supply chain. 
However, many researchers have realised that despite this fact there are disparate results between 
companies investing in supply chain technology where some are for example delivering new products 
to the market in record time while others are struggling to manage inventory (Randall, Morgan, & 
Mortin, 2003). This has led many researchers to believe that the long-term competitive advantage of 
supply chain investments is not resulting from investments of technology alone, but also with the 
alignment between the supply chain strategy and the nature of products (Fisher 1997; de Groote 1994; 
Milogram and Roberts 1990).  
Fisher (1997) pointed out when answering the question “how to manage supply chains when operations 
compete in different ways in different markets”, that apparently companies have similar products which 
compete in different ways. In addition to this Fisher developed a matrix, the Fisher’s Product-Supply 
Chain Matrix, where products are categorized into two basic products: functional and innovative. The 
major differentiating factor is the uncertainty of demand where functional products have a stable demand 
and innovative an unstable demand. According to the matrix, functional products are better suited for 
an efficient supply chain while the innovative products for a more responsive one (Slack, Brandon-
Jones, & Johnston, 2013), which can be useful knowledge in many industries. However, there is limited 
amount of literature regarding supply chains in the maritime industry specifically, which indicates a 
research gap when connecting supply chain management to that specific industry. The maritime industry 
has unlike other industries a moving destination point, i.e. the vessels, which makes this topic interesting. 
1 
 
==PAGE:17==
 
Introduction 
 
 
 
Some researchers have pointed out that Fisher’s model does not indicate uncertainty in many areas such 
as supply uncertainty from a raw material side (Lee, 2002; Ho et al., 2005) or manufacturing uncertainty 
resulting from lead time variations in the manufacturing process (Ho et al., 2005). This thesis aims to 
investigate the uncertainty that a moving destination point entails and whether or not it supports Fishers 
theory by using a case company to carry out the research.  
1.2 The Research Question 
The investigation starts with a formulation of a research question which is used as a focal point in this 
thesis. In order to address the question the study investigates existing theories and models in the supply 
chain management field and conducts an empirical study in order to apply the existing theory and 
attempt to bridge the research gap. The research question that this thesis addresses is the following:  
 
What is the relevant supply chain strategy for a product in the maritime industry with a moving 
destination point? 
1.3 Research Objectives and Aim 
When deciding on a supply chain strategy there are several aspects that need to be considered, such as 
where to manufacture the product, how to deliver, where to keep warehouses, if the company should 
outsource etc. The aim of this thesis is to obtain a broader knowledge of supply chain management 
within the maritime industry where the final destination is moving and obtain the relevant supply chain 
strategy for products used on board vessels. Furthermore, the authors would like to contribute a 
framework or a route map for companies to use in similar situations with the same goal.   
1.4 Scope of the Project  
Traditionally, a supply chain is built up by a supplier, manufacturer, storage facility, transportation, 
distribution centres and retailers (Beamon, 1998), where the main activities include purchasing, 
distribution management, logistics, material management and customer relationship management 
(Slack, Brandon-Jones, & Johnston, 2013). In this report the supply chain is considered as a whole entity 
but will focus more in depth on the inventory control and distribution & logistics, with emphasise on 
storage facility, transportation and the end user, in order to limit the magnitude of the project.  In Figure 
1 the scope of the supply chain is illustrated where the focus area is highlighted. Note that the end user 
is considered to include inventory on board the vessels, which is however considered out of scope. 
Additionally, raw material suppliers, manufacturing processes and social & environmental issues are 
out of scope in this project.  
 
Figure 1: The supply chain process (Adjusted from Beamon (1998)). 
2 
==PAGE:18==
 
Introduction 
 
 
 
1.5 Thesis Overview 
The thesis overview is demonstrated in Figure 2, including a brief description of the content of each 
chapter. A summary is made after each chapter to gather the most important information.

==PAGE:19==
 
Introduction 
 
 
 
1.6 Summary 
Researchers such as Fisher (1997), de Groote (1994) and Milogram & Roberts (1990) identified that 
aligning supply chain strategies and the nature of products is essential to achieve long-term competitive 
advantage. The result was a supply chain matrix developed by Fisher (1997), in order to provide a 
framework for managers when choosing a supply chain strategy for their products. According to the 
matrix, functional products are better suited for an efficient supply chain while innovative products call 
for a more responsive one (Slack, Brandon-Jones, & Johnston, 2013), which can be a useful knowledge 
in many industries.  However, a limited amount of literature regarding supply chain strategies in the 
maritime industry specifically was identified, which indicates a research gap when connecting supply 
chain management to the maritime industry. The maritime industry is different from other industries in 
terms of the destination point. The destination point is moving, which makes this topic interesting. This 
thesis aims to investigate the uncertainty a moving destination point entails and answer the research 
question: “what is the relevant supply chain strategy for a product in a maritime industry with a moving 
destination point?” by carrying out an empirical case study on a company in the maritime industry. A 
research methodology and data analysis based on the theoretical foundations proposed in the literature 
review of this thesis, will be used throughout the study. This data study will answer the research question 
of the project and conclude recommendations for the case company, as well as propose further work in 
the field. The next chapter provides the literature review and the theoretical foundations necessary to 
carry out the study and developing the project. 
4 
==PAGE:20==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
2. Literature Review  
This chapter covers the central theoretical background of the research for this project. It starts with an 
introduction to Supply Chain Management, discussions on different supply chain strategies and 
inventory management, as well as case studies from different industries to give an empirical example of 
the theory discussed in the chapter. Hypotheses are developed at the end of this chapter from the 
literature findings. Theories that are considered relevant for this report will be presented and their 
usability will then be evaluated and discussed in Chapter 6. 
2.1 Supply Chain Management  
The hard competition in today’s global markets and the customers’ high expectations has made 
enterprises invest more in their supply chains (Handfield & Ernest, 1999). Traditionally, companies 
consider themselves as entities with an independent existence that are competing with others to survive 
in the market. In the contemporary industrial setup the competition is not between companies, instead 
supply chains are competing with other supply chains (Christopher, 2011). A supply chain has been 
defined as “the network of organizations that have upstream and downstream linkages for organizations 
that either procure or produce tangible products, or services which reach the end-users, i.e. the customer” 
(Singh, 2014), illustrated in Figure 3.  Many world-class organizations have realized this situation and 
strive to make their supply chains more efficient, responsive and competitive. Supply Chain 
Management is essentially a management of relation, coordination and trust among all partners of the 
supply chain and firms realise the benefits of cooperative relationships within and outside their 
organisations and see that they no longer compete effectively in isolation. (Singh, 2014). 
 
Figure 3: The main stages of a supply chain (Singh, 2014). 
According to Narashimha et al. (2007) the objective of supply chain management is to be cost-effective 
and efficient across the whole supply chain and it should consider everything that has an effect on cost, 
from start of the chain to the end, i.e. from the supplier and manufacturer facilities through warehouses, 
distribution centres to retailers and stores. It is a cost effective way of managing materials, information 
and financial flow from the point of origin to the point of consumption to satisfy customer demand 
(Narasimha & Roy, 2007).   
Handfield & Ernest (1999) identified three main things that make Supply Chain Management complex:  
 
Supply chain strategies cannot be determined in isolation; everything is affected by the 
development chain, i.e. all activities developing a new product. 
 
Designing a whole supply chain by minimising the total cost and yet keeping the same service 
level is challenging. 
 
The supply chain needs to be designed in a way that risks and uncertainties are reduced since 
they are the fundamental issues of a supply chain. 
5 
==PAGE:21==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
Supply chain design attempts to create the appropriate supply chain for a company’s operating 
environment. When deciding how to configure a new product supply chain, the main question is to 
determine which parts and processes should be selected (Willems, 1999). For example, various vendors 
can supply a certain raw material, multiple machines or processes can manufacture the assembly and 
multiple shipping options can deliver the completed product to the final customer. Each of these different 
options is differentiated by its production time and direct cost added (Willems, 1999). 
It is important to be aware of that any organisation can be part of many supply chains. For example 
AT&T, American multinational telecommunications corporation, might find Motorola to be a customer 
in one supply chain, a partner in another, a supplier in a third and a competitor in a fourth supply chain 
(Mentzer, et al., 2001). With supply chain management philosophy companies need to establish 
management practices to behave consistently with the philosophy. According to Mentzer et al. (2006) 
the activities that are required to successfully implement a SCM philosophy in to supply chains are: 
 
Integrated behaviour. 
 
Mutually sharing information. 
 
Mutually sharing risks and rewards. 
 
Cooperation. 
 
The same goal and the same focus on serving customers. 
 
Integration of processes. 
 
Partners to build and maintain long-term business relationships. 
2.2 Supply Chain Management in the Maritime Industry 
As Chapter 1 identified, there is limited amount of literature regarding supply chain management in the 
maritime industry. However, several articles have been written regarding maritime logistics and supply 
chains, as summarised in Table 1 .The articles involve port logistics, collaboration and other parts of the 
maritime industry. Nicholson (2006) published a thesis about Supply Chain Management in the Bulk 
Shipping Industry, with the objective to improve the supply chain by maximising customer service while 
minimizing cost. The thesis focuses though only on the dry bulk industry and how the supply chain can 
be improved without introducing other supply chain partners. These specific articles however do not 
comprehend the same research area and were not found applicable for this study, since the objective is 
to find the relevant supply chain strategy for companies in the maritime industry and see the affect a 
moving destination point has on strategic decision making. 
A thorough investigation was made by the authors of this report regarding the destination point of a 
supply chain to see if there is an existing literature on this matter, i.e. if the location is important, or the 
fact that it is moving influences the structure of the supply chain. Material was found regarding 
distribution centres, which indicates that the location can play a significant role in whether the supply 
chain should be responsive or efficient (Dittmann, et al., 2011). That is however not considered the end 
user which this project focuses on. No articles or cases were found regarding a supply chain where the 
end user is a moving destination point, which indicates the research gap this report addresses.

2.3 Supply Chain Strategies 
In this section the different supply chain strategies are discussed. The two main functions within supply 
chains and different business processes are presented in order to clarify these terms used later in the 
chapter. Fisher’s matrix is presented with empirical examples from each quadrant. Moreover, hybrid 
strategies including lean and agile paradigms are discussed.  
2.3.1 Supply Chain Functions 
A supply chain performs two different types of functions; a physical function and market mediation 
function (Singh, 2014) as described in Table 2. The cost included in the physical function is a tangible 
cost but the market mediation cost arises when supplies exceed demand or when supply falls short of 
demand, resulting in lost sales and dissatisfied customers. Fisher (1997) pointed out that if the risk of 
obsolescence or the cost of stockout is higher in comparison to the production cost and distribution cost, 
then a different supply chain strategy should be used. This leads to the conclusion that the supply chain’s 
total Product Delivery Process (PDP) cost can be expressed with equation 1 (Mason-Jones, Naylor, & 
Towill, 2000).

2.3.2 Business Processes in Supply Chain Strategies 
In the field of operations management production forms are generally divided into five generic types. 
The fundamental issue for the separation in these production forms is whether materials and products 
should be made to stock or made to order (Hansen, 2003). The role of the customer is essential in these 
decisions. The point where the customer is included in the process is referred to as the customer order 
decoupling point (CODP), which is in other words the dividing line between production made to order 
and production made to stock (Hvam, Mortensen, & Riis, 2008) . 
Make-to-stock refers to processes where materials and products are produced to be stored as finished 
products (Hansen, 2003). In these processes the customer is introduced after the product has been made, 
i.e. the CODP is after fabrication and assembly as shown in Figure 4. Traditionally, sales function picks 
the finished goods directly from stock, which results in fast delivery to the customer. However, in this 
scenario the possible inaccuracy in sales forecasts is a risk factor that could result either in stockout or 
excess in inventory (Hansen, 2003). The customer’s decision on whether or not to buy a product made 
to stock is usually based on the choice that the retailer can offer at that particular moment, thus if a 
product is out of stock the customer can easily turn to the competitor (Hansen, 2003). An example of 
make to stock could be Wal-Mart products.   
Make-to-order refers to processes where the entire production of materials and products takes place after 
a customer orders (Hansen, 2003), i.e. the CODP is before the fabrication and assembly of the product, 
shown in Figure 4. These products are found in a wide variety. Delivery times are often long since 
production time takes place between an order and final shipment. By making to order, risk is mitigated 
7 
==PAGE:23==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
since all resources have an economic justification and inventory cost is reduced (Hansen, 2003). An 
example could be tailor made clothes. 
The combination of both principles from make-to-stock and make-to-order is used in manufacturing 
processes and is called assemble-to-order. In these circumstances some parts are made to stock, perhaps 
some shared parts that are used in all products within a given product family. These shared parts are also 
called standard parts or modules.  The rest of the components that make the product unique from the 
other products within the same family are made to order (Hvam, Mortensen, & Riis, 2008). This means 
that the CODP is before the assembly of the product but after fabrication, see Figure 4. Products that are 
assembled-to-order are standard but they can also be customised. When products are personalised for a 
customer it is essential to deliver them on time, whilst minimizing cost. These requirements are easier 
to fulfil if the customer’s order decoupling point is postponed (Hansen, 2003). The aim of postponement 
is to increase the efficiency of the supply chain by moving product differentiation closer to the customer 
(Naylor, Naim, & Berry, 1999). An example could be Dell computers. Dell manufactures some standard 
parts of its computers up to the customer order decoupling point, where the customer customises the 
computer after his desires.  
Figure 5 shows a comparison of the variety of products resulting from these process types, i.e. 100% 
make-to-stock yields in no product variety and 100% make-to-order yields in high product variety. This 
indicates that the closer the processes are to make-to-order principles the more responsive the supply 
chain has to be as Table 4 in the next section shows.   
 
Figure 4: The customer order decoupling point (Hvam, 
Mortensen, & Riis, 2008). 
Figure 5: Product variety within each 
business with regards to MTO and 
MTS (Hansen, 2003) .

2.3.3 Fisher’s Matrix 
Fisher (1997) said the main cause of problems in supply chains is the mismatch between the type of 
product and the type of supply chain and stated that managers were facing too many issues in their 
supply chains due to the lack of framework. Fisher concluded that if a company would choose a supply 
chain on the basis of its demand pattern the appropriate supply chain strategy could be chosen. Fisher 
developed a framework better known as Fisher’s matrix to resolve this. The matrix divides product types 
to either primarily functional or primarily innovative. Because of the differentiation between these 
product types each category requires a different supply chain, either physically efficient or responsive 
to market. In Figure 6 the match and mismatch between these two dimensions are illustrated. 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 6: Fisher’s Product-Supply Chain Matrix (Fisher, 1997) 
 
As Table 3 demonstrates, there is a distinctive difference between functional and innovative products. 
The main difference consists in the demand pattern, where functional products have a steady demand 
and innovative products an unsteady demand (Singh, 2014). Functional products often entail low profit 
margins since their stability invites competition (Singh, 2014) and the price sensitivity, i.e. the customer 
demands a reasonable price for the product (Hayes & Wheelright, 1979), makes it easy for the customer 
to turn to the competitor if that requirement is not fulfilled. Low profit margins are the reason companies 
introduce innovative products, where companies often bring innovation to an already existing functional 
product (Fisher, 1997).  Innovative products are products that have an extra factor that gives the 
customer an additional reason to buy it, common in the fashion and IT industries. This type of products 
is also found within traditional functional categories of food, where companies have tried innovative 
concepts (Fisher, 1997). However, this categorization shouldn’t be considered as mutually exclusive for 
each type of product. For example, clothes bought in Wal-Mart are functional products while fashion 
clothes such as Zara can be classified as innovative (Chopra & Meindl, 2012). Computers, cars and ice 
cream, to name a few, can both be offered as a functional product or in an innovative form. Imitators 
repeatedly demolish the competitive advantage of innovative products and therefore companies are 
forced to introduce a steady stream of innovations (Fisher, 1997). 
Functional 
Innovative  
Match
Mismatch
Mismatch
Match
Supply chain 
Product  
Responsive

The steady demand for functional products makes it possible to forecast accurately, and therefore it is 
possible to obtain a close match of supply and demand (Fisher, 1997). This makes the market mediation 
part of the supply chain, discussed in Section 2.3.1, easier since match in supply and demand indicates 
few stockouts and excess inventory. This gives companies with functional products the chance to focus 
merely on minimizing physical cost, which is a crucial goal considering the price sensitivity for most 
functional products (Hayes & Wheelright, 1979). According to Hernán (2013), an efficient supply chain 
is best fit for industries where there is intense market competition, with several competitors fighting for 
the same customer group due to the lack of differences in value proposals and competition is merely 
based on the price, i.e. functional products. This supports what Fisher’s matrix in Figure 6 demonstrates. 
The large batch sizes enable the efficient supply chain to produce at a low unit cost, but frequently at 
the expense of market responsiveness (Randall, Morgan, & Mortin, 2003). Supplier, manufacturer and 
retailers manage their activities in order for supply to meet the predictable demand through independent 
ordering processes between the different stages of the supply chain (Fisher, 1997).  The unsteady 
demand for innovative products can entail a bullwhip effect upstream the supply chain (Fisher, 1997). 
A bullwhip effect refers to the tendency of bigger and bigger swings in inventory in response to changes 
in demand, as one stage in the supply chain looks further up the supply chain for a product (Singh, 
2014).  In order to counteract this effect the supply chain has to be responsive, as Fishers’s matrix in 
Figure 6 illustrates. The small batch sizes often come at a higher unit cost but it enables the responsive 
firm to quickly adapt to market demand (Randall, Morgan, & Mortin, 2003).  
Improving a supply chain and making it more responsive entails cost. This is because safety stock and 
inventories need to be maintained and short lead-times are essential, so investments are necessary in 
order to reduce lead-time (Minnich & Maier, 2006). A fact that companies have discovered is that the 
economic gain from investments in improving supply chain responsiveness, for a company with an 
innovative product, are greater than the rewards from investments in improving the chain’s efficiency 
(Fisher, 1997). A responsive supply chain, on the contrary to efficient supply chains, receives its 
information flow from the market to supply chain members with the goal to hedge inventory and 
available production capacity against uncertain demand (Fisher, 1997). The two types of supply chain 
strategies are compared in Table 4.

2.3.3.1 Examples from each quadrant 
Realigning a product and a supply chain strategy is not easy but “the reward is a remarkable competitive 
advantage that generates high growth in sales and profits and makes the effort worth it” (Fisher, 1997). 
Fisher gave empirical examples from each quadrant of his matrix. These examples are demonstrated in 
this chapter in order to provide a greater understanding of the problems companies could be facing.  
2.3.3.1.1 An efficient supply chain of functional products  
Campbell Soup Company, a global food company with annual sales of more than $8 billion (Campbell's, 
n.d.), showed how to supply a functional product in an efficient way, summarized in Table 5. In order 
to pursue physical efficiency the company introduced an electronic data interchange (EDI) system, 
which was established with their most progressive retailers. The retailer informed the company each day 
about inventory levels and demand. This made it easier for the company to forecast future demand and 
determine which products required refilling, which were then delivered to the retailer’s inventory the 
same day. By this cooperation and by cutting down delivery lead-time the company managed to lower 
the inventories of retailers from four weeks to two weeks and stock availability increased from 98,5% 
to 99,2%. Thus, the gain for Campbell was an increased efficiency through reduction in inventory of the 
retailer. This results in a win-win situation between the retailer and the manufacturer, i.e. it is beneficial 
for the retailer to sell products from suppliers with the lowest cost to stock and at the same time retailers 
are more likely to want to give the supplier more shelf space and perhaps carry a broader line of products.

2.3.3.1.2 A responsive supply chain of a functional products 
According to Fisher (1997), It is rare for companies that introduce a functional product to structure a 
responsive supply chain. Usually companies realize that a functional product requires an efficient supply 
chain.  
2.3.3.1.3 An efficient supply chain of innovative products 
Many companies focus on physical efficiency in their supply chain, even though they are marketing an 
innovative product. The reason is that the introduction of new products has increased and therefore many 
companies try and change their functional products into innovative products to thrive. This is on the 
contrary to Fisher’s theory, i.e. unresponsive supply chains trying to supply innovative products is a 
mismatch. In Table 6 an example of a company in the computer industry twenty years ago showed that 
a company could supply an innovative product with an unresponsive process if the market accepts long 
lead-time for delivery. 
Table 6: IBM managed to match innovative product with an efficient supply chain strategy.

to organize the supplies in an efficient way 
 
However, in the industry today the customer demands a delivery lead-time in the count of days. 
Therefore, companies that emphasise an efficient supply chain with an innovative product should either 
(Fisher, 1997):  
 
Make their supply chain responsive: It is dependent on whether the product is innovative enough 
to create profit to cover the cost of making the supply chain responsive, since the change requires 
investments, e.g. to ensure decreased lead-time.

 
Make their products functional: This is a solution if the products are characterized by frequent 
introductions of new products, variety is high but it is resulting in low profit margins. For 
example, Procter & Gamble sold toothpaste in 28 different types, which was concluded not 
necessary and a move to the left quadrant of Fishers matrix appropriate. 
2.3.3.1.4 Responsive supply chain of innovative products 
The challenge with innovative products is to figure out how to create a responsive supply chain. National 
Bicycles is a small division of Matsushita Electric, better known as Panasonic Corporation, a 
multinational electronics corporation. As illustrated in Table 7, when National Bicycles aligned their 
product with their supply chain strategy their market share increased. So, instead of competing with the 
other functional bikes National Bicycles decided to focus on their innovative segment. By avoiding the 
high risk of overproduction due to the short life cycle and uncertain demand from these products they 
managed to increase their share of sports bikes from 5% to 29%.  This was obtained by using mass 
customization1 with a configure-to-order set-up, which is similar to assemble-to-order set-up. Even 
though the demand was unpredictable, with this set up they managed to match supply and demand as 
the customer ordered.  
Table 7: National Bicycles developed a responsive supply chain for their innovative product.

2.3.4 Hybrid Strategies: an Extension to Fisher’s Model 
Fisher’s model has been tested by a great deal of researchers since 1997, a summary of some of them 
are in Table 8.  Lo and Power (2010) performed an empirical study with the aim to answer the research 
question “Does Fisher’s (1997) model represent the association between product nature and supply 
network strategy appropriately?” by using Fisher’s model as a framework. The study was conducted 
through a survey, with 107 companies as the survey group. The results indicated that companies do not 
focus on the nature of the product when selecting a supply chain strategy and a great deal of them use a 
combination of both efficient and responsive strategies, i.e. a hybrid strategy, irrelevant to the product’s 
nature. The authors argued that probably the study did not support Fisher’s framework due to the 
possibility of change in characteristics of supply chain strategies in the past decade (Lo & Power, 2010).  
Before this study was conducted there were researchers that partly supported Fisher’s matrix. One 
research indicated an association between efficient strategy and functional products but the relationship 
1  Mass customization: “a production form in which customized products are delivered which exploit the 
advantages of mass production” (Hvam, Mortensen, & Riis, 2008). 
13 
                                                     
==PAGE:29==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
between innovative products and responsive supply chains was not supported (Sellding & Olhager, 
2007). The outcome of another study indicated the opposite, a link between innovative products and 
responsive manufacturing but not between efficient supply chains and functional products (Li & 
O'Brien, 2001).  
Achieving responsiveness and efficiency at the same time in a supply chain is a hard task. Since 
increased responsiveness can entail decreased efficiency, and vice versa, the goal of achieving both at 
the same time typically involves trade-off decisions by management (Minnich & Maier, 2006). Bearing 
in mind that responsiveness encourages higher safety stock while efficiency encourages lower safety 
stocks (Minnich & Maier, 2006), an example of a trade-off between responsiveness and efficiency could 
be a deliberate increase in safety stock, increasing the responsiveness through product availability. 
However, this increases both storage cost and indirect cost (product on stock might not be sold), reducing 
the level of efficiency.  
According to Minnich and Maier (2006), Fisher’s principle ideas of aligning the product nature to the 
appropriate supply chain strategy are reasonable. However, the interrelatedness suggests that actions to 
improve efficiency, e.g. investments in manufacturing cycle time reduction could at the same time lead 
to an improved response in the supply chain (Minnich & Maier, 2006). For example, a company 
producing precision servo valves used lean methods to eliminate waste and thereby increasing 
efficiency. In order to separate problems in production, selected inventory buffers were increased while 
all other inventory buffers were reduced, increasing efficiency. This resulted in more responsiveness to 
the customer with improved service (Hopp & Spearman, 2004).  
Table 8: Authors that have tested Fisher's model and their conclusions. 
Authors 
Conclusion 
(Lo & Power, 2010)  
 Fisher’s model not supported 
 The strategy should not be chosen by the product nature 
 Hybrid strategy is the most common use by companies 
(Minnich & Maier, 
2006) 
 Fisher’s model is reasonable 
 There is a trade-off between responsiveness and efficiency 
 Efficiency does not have a negative impact on responsiveness 
(Sellding & Olhager, 
2007) 
 Association between efficient strategy and functional products 
 Relationship between innovative products and responsive supply 
chain not supported 
(Li & O'Brien, 2001) 
 Link between innovative products and responsive manufacturing  
 No link between functional products and efficient supply chains 
The coexistence of strategies, i.e. a hybrid strategy, is explained as a leagile system. This is because 
both efficient and responsive strategies can be matched with lean and agile paradigms respectively (Lo 
& Power, 2010).  
2.3.4.1 Lean, Agile and Leagile systems  
Two important paradigms in supply chain management today are lean and agile. These concepts are 
used in order to achieve efficiency and responsiveness (Lo & Power, 2010).  
The lean paradigm includes maximizing customer value while intending to eliminate waste, i.e. non-
value adding activities, and aims to achieve reduction in cost and moderate speed & flexibility, similar 
to the focus of efficient supply chains. Demand should be stable and a level production schedule should 
be used. A level schedule is crucial in order to eliminate waste and thereby minimize physical cost and 
maximize profit (Mason-Jones, Naylor, & Towill, 2000). The agile paradigm includes responding 
rapidly to unpredictable changes in demand (Christopher, 2011). Agile strategy aims to increase speed 
14 
==PAGE:30==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
and flexibility, where cost is not a crucial criterion (Myerson, 2014), matching the responsive supply 
chain strategy, where the goal is to have it agile (Christopher, 2011).  
2.3.4.1.1 Market Qualifiers and Market Winners 
Hill (1993) developed the concept of order winners and order qualifiers in order to help determine the 
right manufacturing strategy. Order qualifier is the criteria that a company must meet in order for a 
customer to consider it to be a possible supplier, and order winner is the criteria required in order to win 
orders from customers. This idea can be further developed into a wider supply chain oriented concept 
of market qualifiers and market winners, not merely focusing on the appropriate manufacturing strategy 
but rather on the appropriate supply chain strategy (Christopher & Towill, 2001). This is further 
illustrated in Figure 7 for lean and agile respectively. 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 7: Market qualifiers and market winners for lean and agile paradigms respectively (Mason-
Jones et al., 2000; Lo & Power, 2010). 
Lean and agile paradigms are essentially different but have been combined within successfully designed 
and operated supply chains (Mason-Jones, Naylor, & Towill, 2000) and many researchers think the 
combination of both agile and lean is beneficial, e.g. Mason-Jones & Towill (2000) and Myerson (2014). 
The volatile in market demand is a key point in agility and therefore, it is crucial for businesses in supply 
chains to exploit this volatility to their strategic advantage. Customer service level, i.e. the availability 
of a product, is a market winner in a volatile marketplace as Figure 7 illustrates. However, cost is a 
crucial market qualifier, which is usually reduced by using lean ideology (Mason-Jones, Naylor, & 
Towill, 2000). The solution could therefore be to apply the leagile supply chain paradigm illustrated in 
Figure 8. Using lean and agile strategies together is known as using a hybrid supply chain strategy 
(Myerson, 2014), as stated before and the definition of leagility is: “the combination of the lean and 
agile paradigm within a supply chain strategy by positioning the decoupling point so as to best suit the 
need for responding to a volatile demand downstream yet providing level scheduling upstream from the 
decoupling point” (Mason-Jones, Naylor, & Towill, 2000). 
Market Qualifiers Market Winners

Cost
Lead-Time
Service Level

Agile/Responsive 
15 
==PAGE:31==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
 
Figure 8: Lean, Agile and leagility system (Mason-Jones, Naylor, & Towill, 2000). 
As Figure 7 illustrates, both agility and leanness require high product quality in order for the customer 
to even consider buying the product. Low lead-time is also essential for both paradigms. Since the 
demand is unpredictable and volatile the lead-time should be minimized to enable agility. Long lead-
time of a product causes difficulties in the supply chain to respond quickly to the change in demand 
(Mason-Jones, Naylor, & Towill, 2000). Lean aims to eliminate all waste, including excess time and 
therefore lead-time has to be minimized. The essential difference between the two supplies in terms of 
value to the customer is that service level is critical for agility but sales price for lean (Mason-Jones, 
Naylor, & Towill, 2000).  
2.3.4.1.2 Lean and agile supply  
As stated above (in Section 2.3.1), a supply chain’s total PDP cost is the sum of the physical PDP cost 
and market mediation cost. Here it can be said that the physical PDP cost source is dominated by lean 
supply and market mediation cost source by agile supply (Christopher & Towill, 2001).  A comparison 
of the two paradigms is illustrated in Table 9.

From Table 10 it can be considered that a supply chain using both paradigms would be an optimal 
situation (Naylor, Naim, & Berry, 1999). However, according to Naylor et al. (1999) the whole set of 
characteristics for these paradigms differentiate in a way that they cannot be used at the same point in 
any supply chain. If customer’s demand is unpredictable and out of the supply chains control it is not 
possible to implement lean. The sudden change in demand would result in waste in producing near 
capacity or due to the need for larger safety stocks (Naylor, Naim, & Berry, 1999). Therefore, the 
paradigm chosen for a specific supply chain depends on the customer’s demand.  
2.3.4.1.3 Lean and agile paradigms connected with the different strategies 
The make-to-order, assemble-to-order and make-to-stock business processes discussed earlier can be 
connected with the lean and agile paradigms and the customer order decoupling point, see Figure 9. The 
decoupling point separates the supply chain into two parts (Naylor et al.):  
 
The one where the supply chain responds directly to the customer. 
 
The other one that uses forward planning and a strategic inventory buffer to cope with the 
uncertain demand. 
Therefore, the positioning of the customer order decoupling point depends on the maximum lead time a 
customer is prepared to accept and the point where variability in product demand dominates.  
Downstream from the point the supply chain is market driven, i.e. all products are pulled by the end 
user, and upstream the supply chain is initially forecast driven (Mason-Jones, Naylor, & Towill, 2000). 
Naylor et al. (1999) related agile and lean manufacturing paradigms to supply chain strategies by saying 
that the lean paradigm can be applied upstream from the decoupling point, where the demand is steady 
and standard products flow through a number of value streams2. The agile paradigm can then be applied 
downstream where the demand is variable and the product variety per value stream is wider (Mason-
Jones, Naylor, & Towill, 2000). The three types of supply chain strategies are compared in Figure 9.

Dictionary)

paradigms are respectable and within the right supply chain the combination of them is complimentary.

2.3.5 Information Enriched Supply Chain 
In a traditional supply chain the retailer is the stage that has a direct overview of customer’s demand. 
All other stages observe the orders from the next stage to them downstream the supply chain, for instance 
the warehouse only has sight of the orders from the distributor (Mason-Jones, Naylor, & Towill, 2000). 
This can entail a distortion in information by the retailer and the further upstream it goes, the more 
distortion (Mason-Jones & Towill, 1997). An information enriched supply chain is a concept where 
each stage in the supply chain, no matter how far upstream, receives market data directly. The 
18 
==PAGE:34==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
mechanism is an electronic point of sales link (Mason-Jones & Towill, 1997), which should increase 
transparency throughout the chain and prevent data misrepresentation.  In lean supply, the information-
enriched concept is desirable while in agile supply it is obligatory.  
No matter which paradigm is applied to a supply chain all stages must work together where the focus 
fulfils the customer’s demand. This means that even though the terminology is different the essence is 
the same (Naylor, Naim, & Berry, 1999). The goal of an integrated supply chain is to ease the flow of 
material, cash, resources and information. Figure 10 illustrates the move from a functional silo-
structured supply chain to an integrated supply chain (Stevens, 1989). Integrating the supply chain will 
simplify, streamline and reduce waste and lead-time of both information and material flow (Naylor, 
Naim, & Berry, 1999). 
 
 
Figure 10: Supply chain integration (Stevens, 1989).

2.5 Other Supply Chain Considerations  
There are other supply chain considerations than thoes mentioned in previous chapters. This section 
considers only a few of them and the onther factors. It focuses on the ones which are most related to the 
research question and therefore may help analysing the case.  
2.5.1 Push vs. Pull Strategy  
When deciding on how to structure the supply chain a push or a pull strategy has to be considered. Push 
strategy depends on forecasts in order to have the right products and the right amount of product on 
inventory to serve the customer. If the manufacturer is seen as the furthermost upstream point in the 
supply chain, the raw materials are pushed through the production systems, the products produced are 
pushed into the warehouses and from the warehouses, they are pushed into the distribution channels and 
further to the retailer/customer. The push ordering structure is described as a centralised decision making 
without direct data from the marketplace (Hinkelman & Putzi, 2005). In a push-based supply chain, 
production and distribution decisions are based on long-term forecast. In a pull-based supply chain, 
production and distribution are demand-driven so that they are matched with real customer demand 
rather than forecast demand (Simchi-Levi, Simchi-Levi, & Kaminsky, 2008). This can be connected to 
of the business processes discussed in Section 2.3.2. 
Companies need to decide on what is the most appropriate supply chain strategy for their product. Push-
based strategy, pull-based strategy or push-pull strategy (see Figure 11). The vertical axis shows 
information on uncertainty in customer demand, while the horizontal axis shows the importance of 
economies of scale in either production or distribution. The two dimensions are spanned into four boxes. 
Box I represents industries or products that are characterised by high uncertainty and where economies 
of scale are not important. Box II represents products and industries where uncertainty in demand is 
high but economies of scale are important. Box III represents products that are characterised by low 
demand uncertainty and high economies of scale, and finally box IV represents products characterised 
by low demand uncertainty and low economies of scale (Simchi-Levi et al., 2008). The push and pull 
20 
==PAGE:36==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
strategy depends a lot on how much lead time is required. If the lead times are long it is hard to 
implement a pull strategy, hence a pull strategy is more relevant in a responsive supply chain.  
 
Figure 11: A framework for matching supply chain strategies with products and industries.(Simchi-
Levi et al., 2008). 
2.5.2 Risk Management in the Supply Chain 
The implementation of a global strategy usually leads to higher complexity of supply chains and 
increases the risk (Szuster, 2010). Vulnerabilities are usually more relevant to organizations that operate 
globally and are dependent on others. In such cases the source of disturbances often lies outside the 
scope of control of the particular organization. In order to identify uncertainties it is necessary to identify 
sources of risk and the impacts of different factors on the supply chain. The central performance 
parameters for supply chain processes are minimising risks, avoiding failures and producing quality 
(Haijun, 2008). When selecting suppliers, companies can implement several quality control methods to 
ensure that products meet the specifications contracted while minimising cost and quality-related risks 
(Szuster, 2010). According to Szuster (2010) it is very important to pay a visit to the supplier’s 
manufacturing facility. The company must be audited before manufacturing starts and before the buyer 
places a purchasing order. The evaluation may focus on quality control standards and inspection 
procedures. 
Supply chain risk management is a part of the best practices in the SCOR framework described in 
Section 2.2. The fact that large companies are more exposed to risk has increased by inter-organizational 
networking (Finch, 2004). Many companies’ obsession with speed and costs lead to breakdown of 
supply chains, particularly when a new product is introduced. Although it is impossible to completely 
eliminate risks from a supply chain, it can be reduced. Eleven variables that can impact risk management 
in the supply chain have been chosen by Shankar, Faisal, & Banwet (2006), as illustrated in Table 13. 
Table 13: Variables that can impact risk management in the supply chain.

In order to widen the focus on supply chain weaknesses a supply chain risk profile should be established 
for the business. With the risk profile the aim is to find the critical areas where the management should 
focus its attention. A weakness in this situation is that the supply chain may be most vulnerable where 
the probability of occurrence is small but the potential impact could be giant.  To help identify the risk 
profile of a business it is good to look at the five main sources of risk across the network that are supply 
risk, demand risk, process risk, control risk and environmental risk (Cranfield School of Management, 
2013). These factors are demonstrated in Table 14. It is very important that the management realises 
that the risk profile is directly and indirectly impacted by the strategic decisions it takes. Mapping a risk 
profile can be performed in a few steps as Figure 12 demonstrates.

2.5.2.1 Resilient Supply Chain 
Resilience is a new area of study and therefore we have no concrete definition of a resilient supply 
chains. However, according to the Cranfield School of Management (2013) a resilient supply chain can 
be defined as “the ability of a system to return to its original or desired state after being disturbed”. 
Supply chains are not simply linear chains or processes, they are complex networks (Christopher & 
Peck, 2004). These networks can pose a number of threats that can weaken a supply chain and currently 
many organisations are facing challenges in managing risks (Sheffi, 2005). A new priority has appeared 
for business planning, the search for supply strategies that represent a significantly higher degree of 
resilience. Many organisations lack awareness of the need for considering resilience of their supply 
chains as part of their overall approach to risk and business continuity management (Christopher & 
Peck, 2004). According to Christopher & Peck (2004) it has become increasingly obvious that modern 
supply chains are at greater risk than managers realise. Better management and control of internal 
processes along with more open information flow between organisations is required. 
For many years efficiency was the goal for companies when structuring their supply chains. Now a high 
performing business needs to have something more ambiguous, which is supply chain resilience. With 
early warning about possible risks a company can prepare the right people with the right information 
and action plans that can prevent a disruption or mitigate the impact (Hanifan, 2007). However in reality 
it has not been easy to translate this sound idea into good practice, mostly because people don’t have the 
right tools to systematically analyse resilience yet (OECD, 2014). The reason for that can be that the 
main elements of supply chain resilience are still poorly understood (Ponomarov & Holcomb, 2009). 
The available literature on SC resilience is informative and is primarily focused on presenting several 
theoretical viewpoints of the topic (Sheffi, 2001). However more empirical studies are needed on the 
subject (Bhamra, Dani, & Burnard, 2011), (Wieland & Wallenburg, 2013), (Juttner & Maklan, 2011).  
2.5.3 Outsourcing or In-sourcing  
Offshoring and outsourcing have become options that almost all European engineering companies must 
consider to keep their competitive advantage (Hansen, 2011), where outsourcing is the delegation of 
processes/services to another party, which is defined as the insourcer (Chorafas, 2003). Over the last 
years there has been a tendency to outsource activities that were previously performed within the 
organisation (Christopher, 2011). No part of the value chain has been resistant to this occurrence, as 
companies have for example outsourced distribution, manufacturing, accounting and information 
systems. In some cases these companies might accurately be described as “virtual” companies. The logic 
behind this is based upon that idea that organisations are more likely to succeed if they focus on the 
activities in which they have differential advantage over competitors (Christopher, 2011). 
Factors that motivate globalisation are often a combination of the requirement of being close to 
customers, market access, the availability of cheap labour, reducing transportation costs and delivery 
lead times (Hansen, 2011). However, companies give different reasons for outsourcing their services, 
where reducing cost and focus on core business and improving functional performance seem to be the 
most common (Chorafas, 2003). However, outsourcing increases the likelihood of negative 
consequences for both engineering and management functions. These can include increased cost, delays, 
errors and misunderstandings with consequent lost business opportunities and increased customer 
dissatisfaction (Hansen, 2011). The question whether outsourcing is a good policy is relative since there 
is no general answer (Chorafas, 2003), i.e. each individual case requires thorough studies based on its 
own merits. The principle is, however, that the core function should never be outsourced (Chorafas, 
2003), meaning that if the outsourcing could entail loss of control it should not be pursued. 
Outsourcing can be a strategic move to achieve more responsiveness, and the same applies to insourcing. 
For example, the computer company Compaq decided to continue the production of high variety short 
lifecycle circuits in-house in USA instead of outsourcing and using cheap labour cost in Asia because 
by insourcing the company increased flexibility and reduced lead-time. World Company, a Japanese 
apparel manufacturer, did the opposite. The company has basic styles produced in low-cost plants in 
China but the production of high fashion styles is located in Japan, since the competitive advantage to 
24 
==PAGE:40==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
respond quickly to emerging fashion trends is more important than the disadvantage of high labour costs 
(Fisher, 1997). 
2.5.3.1 Sourcing Process 
The increased global sourcing has put pressure on companies to reduce cost and improve quality in order 
to stay competitive (Gelderman & Semeinj, 2006). The geographical distances in global supply chains, 
makes it more difficult to manage them than domestic supply chains. This set up complicates decisions 
because of inventory cost trade-offs due to the increased lead-time as well as increasing transportation 
cost (Wang, Muhos, Pennanen, & Kess, 2012).   
A Company’s purchasing processes are one of the strategic functions with the highest potential impact 
on long-term profitability (Quentions, Pauwels, & Matthyssens, 2006). Van Weele (2005) described 
purchasing as “the management of the company’s external resources in such a way that the supply of all 
goods, services, capabilities and knowledge which are necessary for running, maintaining and managing 
the company’s primary and support activities is secured at the most favourable conditions”.  
The purchase process is divided into four stages (Quentions, Pauwels, & Matthyssens, 2006):  
 
Investigating the market and evaluating potential suppliers. 
 
Selecting the supplies/service. 
 
The negotiation and contract.  
 
Evaluation of the supplier and follow up. 
This is similar to the sourcing process summarized by Zeng (2003) from other studies involving 
sourcing. The process is illustrated in Figure 13 where the main stages are shown. 
 
 
Figure 13: A generalized five-stage sourcing process  (Zeng, 2003). 
 
25 
==PAGE:41==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
2.5.3.2 Service Level Agreements 
A contract between the company outsourcing its activities and the third party insourcer undertaking the 
activity is called Service Level Agreements (SLA) (Chorafas, 2003). The contract defines deliverables 
and their cost, functionalities, timing and legal procedures, such as dependability of outsource service, 
operational risk, the rights of both parties, resolution of conflicts of interest and contract termination 
(Chorafas, 2003).  
A way to formulate an SLA for the logistics part is to use International Commercial Terms, or Incoterms. 
The International Chamber of Commerce formulated Incoterms in order to reduce dispute between seller 
and purchaser. They are a set of standard trade terms used in purchasing contracts (Leary, 2016), to 
make global trade easier and are designed to help parties in different countries understand one another. 
The different Incoterms are illustrated in Table 71 and Table 72 in Appendix B. 
Parties involved in the transaction select the appropriate terms to determine (Inbound Logistics, 2003):   
 Who is responsible for transportation cost, including insurance, taxes and duties. 
 Who is responsible for loading and unloading of goods. 
 Who bears the risk of loss at each step during the transportation. 
 Where the goods should be picked up and transported. 
2.6 Complexity Management 
Subramanian & Rahman (2013)  defined complexity as “a study of the phenomenon which emerge from 
a collection of interacting objects competing for limited resource”. According to their study on supply 
chain complexity there is no agreed definition of complexity (Subramanian & Rahman, 2013). 
According to Wilson and Perumal (2010), there are three types of complexity within businesses.  
 
Product (and service) complexity: Product complexity describes products or services that are 
offered to a company; a business should try to decrease the number of SKUs3 they have and do 
a SKU rationalization. 
 
Process complexity: Process complexity in the number of processes, handoffs, process steps, 
etc. which are involved in executing and delivering the products. Process complexity often lies 
hidden and outside the range of normal cost-reduction initiatives.  
 
Organizational complexity: Organizational complexity is the number of facilities, assets, 
functional entities, organizational units, systems, etc. involved in executing the processes of a 
company. Organizational complexity has grown at the same pace as process complexity.  
 
Figure 14: Complexity cube (Wilson & Perumal, 2010). 
3 SKUs refers to a specific item stored to a specific location. The SKU is intended as the most disaggregated level 
when dealing with inventory 
26 
                                                     
==PAGE:42==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
Usually when businesses want to reduce complexity they mean cutting product variety or eliminate 
service options. Nevertheless complexity can also appear in the processes and the organization itself 
(people, structures, assets), as the complexity cube illustrates in Figure 14. Each complexity factor 
should be assessed individually as well as collectively when analysing the complexity in a business and 
each complexity factor impacts the other.  Complexity resides on the axis of the cube illustrated in Figure 
14 but the cost arises on the faces of and within the cube (Wilson & Perumal, 2010). 
In order to reduce tangible process complexities in terms of numerousness, differentiation and number 
of interacting pairs the following points should be considered (Narasimha & Roy, 2007): 
 
Reduce number of suppliers and lead time variations through proper supply base rationalization. 
 
Reduce varieties of products. 
 
Insist that suppliers adopt quality policy. 
 
Organize training and evaluation sessions before engaging in long-term collaborative 
relationship. 
 
Focal companies should educate their suppliers about the value of supply chain efficiency. 
 
Focal companies should be aware of hidden costs in warehousing and transportation. 
 
Share profit or losses with suppliers if there is deviation due to currency risk 
 
Only engage with trustworthy suppliers with agree penalties if anything goes wrong. 
Reducing complexity costs is not just about reducing the amount of complexity in the business. It is also 
about reducing the cost of delivering complexity, making complexity less expensive. This means a two-
part approach:  
 
Reduce the overall amount of complexity in the business by removing complexity that adds 
disproportionately large costs to the business. 
 
Reduce the cost impact of each “unit” of complexity on the business by becoming much better 
at managing and delivering it. 
Customers are demanding more products and better services at a lower price but with improved service 
levels. To keep up with competitors requires becoming much better at delivering complexity and good 
performance results in lower cost. If the company can’t do it the competitor will (Wilson & Perumal, 
2010). 
2.7 Inventory Management 
Inventory management is defined by the Business Dictionary as “The group of activates employed in 
maintaining the optimum number of amount of each inventory item to provide uninterrupted production, 
sales, and/or customer-service levels at the minimum cost.” Figure 15 illustrates the supply chain steps 
and different inventories within the chain. Inventory includes raw materials, the work in process and all 
the finished products of a supply chain (Beamon, 1998).  
 
Figure 15: Supply chain steps and different inventory within the chain (Beamon, 1998). 
Companies initiate inventory management to help balance the working capital in inventories and the 
service level, i.e. the percentage of orders satisfied (Simchi-Levi, Simchi-Levi, & Kaminsky, 2008). 
Managing inventory is essentially about balancing supply and demand. Other reasons for holding 
inventory are predictability, fluctuations in demand, unreliability of supply, price protection, quantity 
discounts and lower ordering cost (Muller, 2003). Inventory management is challenging since it directly 
27 
==PAGE:43==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
impacts cost and service. By changing the inventory policies it can lead to dramatic alterations of the 
supply chain’s efficiency and responsiveness (Felea, 2008). Uncertain demand and supply makes it 
necessary to hold inventory at certain places in the supply chain. According to Chopra & Meindl (2007) 
in order to increase efficiency and responsiveness a company must analyse the main types of inventory 
and the way they can be dimensioned. According to Scott et al. (2011), there are several different types 
of inventory as summarised in Table 15. 
Table 15: Different types of inventory (Scott et al., 2011). 
Type of inventory 
Explanation 
Cycle / replenishment stock 
 
The inventory required to meet the normal daily demand 
 
The size of the inventory is the result of production, 
transportation and the acquirement of the goods in large lots 
Safety stock 
 
The buffer against forecast error and supplier’s unreliability 
In-transit stock 
 
Inventory in the process of being transported to a delivery point 
Seasonal stock 
 
The stock built up in advance to meet increase sales volumes 
during a specific time of the year 
Promotional stock 
 
Stock which is fed into marketing campaigns and 
advertisements 
Dead or obsolete stock 
 
Inventory which can no longer be used  or sold 
Although all types of inventory are important, cycle stock and safety stock are the most critical of 
inventory management (Scott, Lundgren, & Thompson, 2011). The problem with inventory is the 
dilemma between the responsiveness that results from a bigger inventory and the efficiency that result 
from a smaller inventory (Felea, 2008). The amount of inventory a company should keep depends on 
the space and working capital required. Keeping little or no stock and negotiation with suppliers to store 
it has some advantages; it is efficient and flexible, the storage cost is lower, and the company is able to 
develop new products or use the working capital for innovation. The disadvantages are that it is more 
expensive having the suppliers keeping the stock; the company is dependent on the efficiency of the 
suppliers and they might run out of stock if the company does not have sufficient control (Scott, 
Lundgren, & Thompson, 2011). 
2.7.1 Economic Order Quantity Formula 
F.W Harris (1913) developed the Economic Order Quantity formula (EOQ), which helps determine the 
amount of products to buy. With the formula and its variations a business can determine the optimal 
quantity order which minimises the total holding and ordering cost (Harris, 1913).  The higher the order 
quantity, the greater the inventory level. However, the higher the order quantity, the fewer times it is 
necessary to order, resulting in lower ordering cost (Frazelle, 2002). The formula is illustrated in the 
following equation (2). 
 
𝐸𝐸𝐸𝐸𝐸𝐸= ඨ2𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴
𝑃𝑃2𝐾𝐾 
 
(2) 
The K factor is the amount it costs to keep an item on stock every day. The R factor is the amount it 
costs to buy an item. The R and K factor affect each other, e.g. if a company buys smaller quantities 
more often the R factor increases but if a company buys larger quantities less often the inventory levels 
are higher for longer period of time which results in the K factor increasing (Muller, 2003).  
F.W Harris (1913)  made some assumptions when determining the lot size formula (Spearman & 
Hoop, 2000). 
 
Production is instantaneous: There is no capacity constraint and the production is 
simultaneous. 
 
Delivery is immediate: No lag between production and availability in order to satisfy the 
demand. 
28 
==PAGE:44==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
 
Demand is deterministic: No uncertainty about quantity or timing of demand.  
 
Constant demand: The demand rate is constant over time.  
 
Fixed setup costs: Regardless of the production lot size or status of the factory, the setup cost 
is the same. 
 
Products can be analysed individually: Products do not share equipment, or there is only 
one product. 
2.7.2 Reorder Point 
The Reorder Point or ROP is the point when a company should put in a purchase order. It is usually the 
lead time demand plus the safety stock, see equation 3 and an illustration of the reorder point and safety 
stock in Figure 16 .An efficient and effective management of inventory throughout the supply chain can 
significantly improve the ultimate service provided to the customer (Lee & Billington, 1992).

2.7.3 Safety Stock Methods 
Safety Stock is to provide acceptable customer service levels when demand is unpredictable during 
replenishment lead times (Frazelle, 2002), i.e. the safety stock must cover the demand variability during 
lead time. A longer lead time leads to higher safety stock (Implement, 2014). Service level is one of the 
most important factors when managing inventories since it is an important variable for calculating safety 
stock (Gunasekaran & Tirtiroglu, 2001). In theory the exact service level can be calculated based on the 
cost of overstocking the product and the cost of being out of stock. The information is however in most 
cases impossible to quantify (Implement, 2014). Therefore, a consideration of the trade-off between 
service level and inventory is made. The factors that most often influence the service level decision are 
predetermined requirement from customer and inventory budget constraints (Gunasekaran & Tirtiroglu, 
2001).Safety stock would be unnecessary if the customer demand, the time of customers order and order 
lead time is known (King, 2011). There are several ways a business can influence the amount kept on 
safety stock. (Scott, Lundgren, & Thompson, 2011). Examples are shown in Table 16. 
Table 16: Actions to reduce safety stock  
Action example 
Result 
Reduce suppliers lead time 
Less safety stock is required 
Reliable suppliers 
Less safety stock is required 
Reduce forecast error 
Less safety stock is required 
Reduce the service level 
Less safety stock is required but results in more 
stockouts, (King, 2011) 
Note: Information in this table is a summary from Scott, Lundgreen & Thompson (2011) 
 
A couple of methods are available to determine the safety stock (King, 2011): 
 
Determining safety stock from demand: Look at the historic demand variability and 
determine how to avoid stockouts, i.e. the percentage of supply cycles that will result in a 
stockout, (see equation 4).  
 
Safety stock based on lead time: Here the safety stock is based on the service level and the 
lead time (see equation 5). 
Taking into consideration standard deviation based on periods equal to total lead time the safety stock 
equation becomes:

2.8 Case Studies in Different Industries  
 In this section, four different cases studies are presented from four different industries. Each of the 
studies is related in some way to the strategies or methods discussed in Chapter 2 of this report and the 
cases are summarised in Table 17. 
2.8.1 Case 1 – Pharmaceutical Industry 
A case study was conducted by Rocha-Lona et al. (2013) on a leading pharmaceutical company in 
Mexico City, responsible for manufacturing medicines for the European market, which was facing 
troubles in the sample shipment process. The process entailed high operation costs due to the company’s 
need to re-send samples if the first shipment was damaged. The low efficient supply chain was resulting 
in waste and high distribution costs. Many complaints in sample shipments from the company’s 
manufacturer site to Europe resulted in the following: impact on cost, low productivity, lack of 
availability to process urgent demands, losses of potential customers and loss of confidence in 
management which had direct impact on current customer satisfaction. In order to eliminate non-value 
adding activities the Lean Six Sigma methodology was used, decreasing both distribution cost and 
process cycle time 
2.8.2 Case 2 –LEAN Manufacturing at Boeing 
The aerospace company Boeing intended in the 1990s to pursue the same methodology in production as 
the car manufacturer Toyota, i.e. lean manufacturing (Anon, 1997). Boeing started using Just In Time 
inventories to help improve their productivity, which enabled it to produce its 737 jet in half the time 
usually. This resulted in lower costs for working capital and higher productivity. In 1995 Boeing was 
producing 200 airplanes a year, but in 1997 the demand for airplanes was over 900 aeroplanes a year, 
to replace ageing noisy aeroplanes. Boeing had not considered the possibility of sudden variations in 
demand and realised that the company had become too lean. In order to respond to increase in demand, 
Boeing had to rehire resources and managed to double its production, but that was not enough to fulfil 
the customer requirement. This gave their main competitor, Airbus Industrie, the possibility to 
successfully increase its market share (Naylor, Naim, & Berry, 1999). 
2.8.3 Case 3 – Activities Outsourced at Boeing 
After losing market share to their main competitor in the 1990s, Boeing could have tried to reduce the 
cost and selling price of the existing aircrafts and let the competitor excel in the innovation competition. 
However, Boeing went in a different direction and started developing the revolutionary 787 Dreamliner 
with the goal to add value for the customer. By using light- weight materials and lightweight batteries, 
Boeing aimed at improving the whole travel experience for the customer. The Dreamliner became the 
fastest selling airplane in aviation history but in 2013 the 787 had problems with the lithium batteries 
that were overheating, and were then grounded (Denning, 2013). 
Complicated products such as aircrafts involve a necessary degree of outsourcing, because the company 
lacks knowledge in some areas. With 787 the goal was to use outsourcing to decrease cost and reduce 
the time of development.  In comparison to former airplane manufacturing Boeing increased outsourcing 
vastly for the 787, or 70% of the airplane. However, the outsourcing did not eliminate cost increase and 
unanticipated technology problems. Sub-contractors were given too much responsibility, and did not 
provide necessary coordination. Boeing had to redesign the sub assembly process, resulting in additional 
cost and the company was outsourcing technology innovations unproven in any aircraft. The outsourcing 
in this case brought profit and knowledge to suppliers while increasing cost for the mother company, 
which lost oversight, since it did not maintain tight control (Denning, 2013). 
 
 
31 
==PAGE:47==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
 
 
2.8.4 Case 4 – Fashion Industry 
Benetton, a global fashion brand, was facing long product cycle times before the 1990s. Benetton’s 
dedication to constant improvements in the supply chain has been a long but rewarding process. In the 
1990s Benetton managed to reduce their new product development phase from 9 months to 5 week cycle 
time for most products, and later to 2 weeks. This time saving was achieved by networked manufacturing 
and postponement in dyeing.  Benetton outsourced intensive production, such as tailoring finishing and 
ironing to local manufacturing networks but kept internal heavy investments strategies and operations 
such as weaving, cutting, dyeing, quality control at all phases and finished goods packaging. 
Postponement of dyeing is a process improvement step Benetton created, which redefined common 
manufacturing processes in the industry. Instead of the usual step: yarn  dye  make  sell, Benetton 
makes for examples sweaters and dyes them once the demand is clear (Feuling, 2007).
