2.9 Supply Chain Processes and Performance Metrics 
According to Closs & Mollenkopf (2004) there is a relationship between supply chain management best 
practices and company’s performance. A framework for companies to achieve outstanding performance 
is to use the Supply Chain Operations Reference (SCOR) model, a management tool used to address, 
improve and communicate supply chain management decisions. The framework defines the essential 
business processes to satisfy customer’s demands and also describes the methods that should be applied 
throughout the entire supply chain and provides a basis for how to improve the processes (Hudson, 
2004). The framework is built on a number of companies’ experience that they have shared in order to 
build a framework of common language that can help improve supply chains. The strength of the model 
is that it provides a standard format to facilitate communication between supply chain partners and 
strengthens the effectiveness of supply chain management, technology and related supply chain 
improvement activities (Huan, Sheoran, & Wang, 2004).  
The benefits of using the framework include efficient supply chains and optimisation. The model is a 
detailed plan for launching new businesses and products and can help a company solve its main 
challenges. The model has four levels that each company has to go through in order to achieve supply 
chain performance improvements (Supply Chain Council, 2010) . The different levels are described in 
Table 18. It is important to understand that the SCOR as a tool should be integrated into existing project 
methodologies used at the company (Supply Chain Council, 2010). 
Table 18: The four levels of the SCOR model that each company has to go through.  
Level 
Description 
Level 1 
The supply chain performance can be directly related to the business objectives 
of the organization (Stephens, 2001). The five management processes are:  
 
Plan processes: The planning activities associated with operating a supply 
chain. Gather customer requirements, collect information on available 
resources and balance requirement and resources in order to determine 
planned capabilities and resource gap. 
 
Source processes: The ordering or scheduling of goods and services. 
 
Make processes: The processes that transform goods into the final 
condition 
 
Deliver processes: The processes that deliver products to the customers 
includes request management, transportation and warehouse management. 
 
Return processes: The processes that return damaged products from the 
customer, this is closely related to the deliver processes. 
Level 2 
Companies implement their operations strategy through the configuration they 
choose for their supply chain, 26 core process categories are defined that are 
possible components of a supply chain. . The organisation or business can build 
their ideal operations using these processes.  
33 
==PAGE:49==
 
Literature Review 
 
 
 
Level 3 
This level provides the information required for successfully planning and 
setting goals for supply chain improvements. This includes defining process 
elements, setting target benchmarks, defining best practices, and system 
software capabilities to enable best practices system or tools. SCOR contains 
185 level 3 processes 
Level 4 
Implementation level, all practices are defined in order to achieve competitive 
advantage and to adapt to changing business conditions. 
Note: Information in this table is summarised from Supply Chain Council (2010). 
2.10 Hypothesis Formulation 
Supply chain management has an extensive amount of literature to search from. However from the 
literature that was considered applicable for this study and discussed throughout this chapter, three 
hypothesis were made:  
1. The products used on board vessels are functional products.  
2. If hypothesis 1 stands, supply chains in the maritime industry should aim for efficiency.  
3. Some degree of a hybrid strategy is applicable when the destination point is moving. 
The first two hypothesis are in line with Fishers matrix, since it is believed that supply chains in the 
maritime industry have functional products on board their vessels and should therefore aim for 
efficiency. However, it is considered that some degree of a hybrid strategy is in order because the 
destination point of the product is on board the vessels, which are moving. This study aims to test these 
hypotheses.    
2.11 Summary  
In this chapter the literature review covers the main concepts and theories of supply chain management.  
Fisher (1997) developed a supply chain strategy matrix, where the concept is to choose the supply chain 
strategy according to the nature of the product in question. A supply chain is either efficient or 
responsive and products either functional with a steady demand or innovative with an unsteady demand. 
Since then many researchers have conducted studies in order to test the matrix, some supporting it and 
others not. From these studies a hybrid supply chain strategy has been developed where both efficiency 
and responsiveness methodology is used in the same supply chains. This is explained with a system 
called leagile strategy, which is a combination of lean and agile paradigms. The leagile strategy focuses 
on the customer order decoupling point, where lean is applicable upstream the supply chain from the 
decoupling point and agile downstream.  
Other supply chain factors are considered such as; risk management, the SCOR framework, along with 
the supply chains importance of resilience to possible risk factors. Outsourcing is discussed as well as 
complexity management and inventory management.  
Four case studies from four different industries are presented in order to give an illustration on how 
some of the concepts discussed in the chapter have been applied in real life situations. However, 
improvement methods as presented in the case studies should always be carefully selected, based on the 
company’s need and experience as well as the industry (Rocha-Lona et al. 2013), in other words the 
solution for one company’s problems is not necessarily the solution for the other.  
In the next chapter the research methodology is described and the research methods used throughout the 
study.

3. Methodology  
This chapter illustrates the research methodology carried out in this thesis and the specific research 
methods used in the different steps of the process to answer the research question proposed in Chapter 
1. The section covers a description of the different research methods, steps in the data gathering process 
and the different data types, which have been used in order to carry out this supply chain management 
problem. 
3.1 Research Process 
The research process used for the report is a five-stage research process presented by Stuart et al. (2002). 
The process can be used for research and dissemination processes and according to Stuart et al. (2002) 
conducting a case research is no different. Case-based research is a scientific approach that attempts to 
connect theoretical ideas with reality (Gubrium, 1988). The model is a generic model of a research 
process and it is used to access how a case-based research approach should be followed (Stuart et al., 
2002). The research process of this report is illustrated in Figure 17 and each step is clarified in Table 
19.

3.2 Research Methods 
The research method, which was decided to use to further investigate the research question, is a case 
study method. The main research methods include experiments, surveys, archival analysis, history and 
case studies. Table 20 describes each research method in relation to the form of the research question, 
whether the approach requires control over behavioural events and if it focuses on contemporary events. 
The case study method was selected since the research question is a “how” question and is exploratory 
in nature (Yin, 1994). “A case study is an empirical enquiry that investigates a contemporary 
phenomenon within its real life context, especially when the boundaries between phenomenon and 
context are not clearly evident” (Yin, 1994). It is particularly appropriate if new fields of research are 
emerging. The advantage of the case study approach is its ability to address “Why?” and “How?” 
questions in the research process.

The number of cases required in a case study depends on the context. Few case studies allow for in-
depth investigations (Vissak, 2010). This feature needs to be balanced against generalizability; the 
higher the number of cases used the higher the degree of generalizability of the findings (Vissak, 2010). 
A single case study is therefore useful when a phenomenon is studied in detail (e.g. a new field) whereas 
multiple case studies are useful when there is some knowledge about the phenomenon but certain 
specific aspects remain unknown and these are then investigated. For this study one case company was 
used since it is applicable for the research question and gives the opportunity to go in depth to further 
investigate the research gap. The case company is the Danish shipping company Maersk and will be 
introduced in Chapter 4. 
When selecting a research method the format of the desired data has to be considered. As Table 21 
presents, external information can be gathered using document analysis, interviews, discourse analysis, 
observations, participant observation and protocol analysis. Information stored in human memory has 
more limitation compared to information stored externally (Ahmed, 2007).

3.2.1 Methods for Literature  
The field of supply chain management has a wide range of literature; however its linkage to the maritime 
industry has a limited range. It was therefore searched for supply chain management in general in order 
to use when studying the case company. For the quality of the project, it was essential to scan a 
substantial array of the comprehensive amount of literature in the field, and carry out in depth studies of 
methods that seemed relevant and applicable in order to obtain a good understanding of the topic. The 
literature review was obtained according to the guidelines described by Rowley and Slack (2004) 
through a citation pearl growing, which starts from one or a few documents and uses any suitable terms 
in those documents to retrieve other documents. The literature search was initiated with a broad search 
of journal articles, conference papers, reports, books, cases and other written material. These were 
searched for in the library database of the Technical University of Denmark, called DTU Findit and 
Google scholar. These resources were also used to trace as many important publications on the subject 
as possible. The usage percentage of each literature type is presented in Table 22.

3.2.2 Methods for the Data Selections Process  
Data collection is an important step in all research studies and its quality impacts the analysis and the 
results of the study. In general, there are defined two types of data; qualitative and quantitative 
(O'Rourke & O'Rourke, 2000).  Qualitative data are characterised by non-numerical value such as 
recorded verbatim. It refers to data that can be observed, but not measured (Patton, 1990). Quantitative 
data is characterised by numerical value such as height, length, volume etc., in other words it is always 
measurable. Both alternatives were important for this project.  
3.2.2.1 Qualitative Data 
The qualitative data was gathered in order to obtain a thorough understanding of the case company’s 
current situation. This data was collected through interviews, email questionnaires and storage facility 
tour in Rotterdam. 
Interview Process  
For this study the interviews were semi-structured and mostly an open format, i.e. the interviewer did 
not know the important points before the interview and the interviews allow for exploration of new 
themes and ideas. The guide was however merely a checklist to see if all relevant points were covered. 
All interviews that were made face-to-face were audio recorded and transcribed and the key points 
gathered. The interviews usually lasted for 40 minutes to an hour. When a face-to-face interview was 
impossible, questionnaires were sent via e-mail and answers analysed. This was for example conducted 
for the delivery companies not located in Denmark and most of the warehouse facilities. 
Observation Process 
In order to obtain information and gain a solid understanding about operational procedures and daily 
activities in warehouses, a factory trip was made to warehouses in Rotterdam and various jobs were 
observed and interviews conducted with different employees. The observation process can be essential 
in order to establish extra tangible knowledge.  
 
 
37 
==PAGE:53==
 
Methodology 
 
 
 
3.2.2.2 Quantitative Data 
Numerical data is required in any study research to ensure objective and accurate results. (Blaikie, 2003). 
The quantitative data was gathered through different platforms: spend data and vessels schedule data 
from the case company, and spreadsheet data from warehouse and transportation companies. 
3.2.2.3 Interviewees Selection 
In order to answer the research question it was important to interview a broad range of people, i.e. top 
managers as well as lower level managers, to gain a multi-perspective view. The interviewees were 
selected based on their relevance and knowledge of the investigated field in order to help answer the 
research question. The interviews usually lasted from 40 minutes to an hour. All the interviews were 
tape-recorded and transcribed and then analysed. Table 23 describes the position, the company, the 
number of interviewees that were interviewed and the frequency of interviews with each interviewee.

3.2.3 Methods for Processing and Analysing Data 
When designing the optimal supply chain it is essential to obtain all necessary data. A great deal of data 
was acquired from the case company, which gave an easy access to required information in order for 
the project to run smoothly. All information gathered from interviews was tape-recorded, transcribed 
and relevant and useful information used in the analysis. Acquiring data from external partners, such as 
warehouses and delivery companies, was more difficult and time consuming. A list of criteria was made 
in order to see which requirements the warehouse facilities and delivery companies had to fulfil. This 
was used when analysing the interviews and e-mails. If a company did not fulfil the criteria it was not 
contacted further. Then the prices and the service level were compared between the different companies 
in order to decide which warehouse and delivery company to use. When analysing the gathered data the 
analysis tool used was Microsoft Excel and when analysing distances Google Map was used. 
Frameworks and methods obtained from literature were also applied.  
3.3 Summary 
The methodology used to carry out the study of this report was the research approach developed by 
Stuart et al. (2002) This methodology consists of five stages, which are developed and applied through 
the different chapters of the thesis. The five stages include development of a research question, 
development in order to capture data for future analysis, collection of data through several platforms, 
analysing the data and finally dissemination of the project. This research approach is applicable for case 
studies, which is relevant for this project since a case company was used in order to answer the research 
question: “What is the relevant supply chain strategy for a product in a maritime industry with a moving 
destination point?”, and Maersk was chosen as the case company.  Multiple forms of data are collected 
in the case study, including interviews, archival records, documentation and direct observation. In the 
next chapter the case company used to carry out this study is presented.

The Collaborative Company 
 
 
 
4. The Collaborative Company 
In order to answer the research question presented in Chapter 1 a case company in the maritime industry 
was used. Maersk Oil Trading was chosen since it meets the criteria for the research question, i.e. the 
company is within the maritime industry and it is about to change its supply chain strategy. Currently 
the company is buying lubricating greases for its vessel fleet from various 3rd party suppliers but it is 
considering changing its strategy and buying the grease directly from the manufacturer. Maersk Oil 
Trading will also own-brand the product and take over stewardship and liability for the product line. 
This would be a completely new setup for the company, i.e. selling its own-branded product. The goal 
of the project is to determine if this is profitable for the company or not.  
Maersk Oil Trading is a part of the Maersk group, a worldwide company operating in 130 countries with 
over 89.000 employees (The Maersk Group, n.d.). It owns the largest container shipping company in 
the world and is involved in a wide range of activities in the shipping, logistics and oil and gas industries. 
In Figure 18 the five main businesses of the Maersk group are illustrated along with other independent 
companies, which are also a part of the Maersk Group (The Maersk Group, n.d.). 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 18: An overview of the various businesses within the Maersk Group.

The Collaborative Company 
 
 
 
4.1.1 History of Maersk Oil Trading Lubricants  
The main activities of Maersk Oil Trading are sourcing of marine fuel for the Maersk Group vessel fleet 
and engagement in trading and hedging of the Group’s oil activities. Maersk Oil Trading consists of 
approximately 50 employees located in offices around the world. The departments within MOT are Fuel 
trading, Business Development, Risk Management, Lubricants, Finance and Business Applications 
(Maersk Oil Trading, n.d.). The main focus of this study is within the highlighted department in Figure 
19, i.e. the lubricant department.  
 
 
 
The lubricant department consist of 11 employees, the team is divided into three areas; operational, 
commercial and technical, and is located both in India and Denmark, see Figure 20. The department 
engages in all areas of the engine lubricant oil industry, from providing technology and procurement 
solutions to market forecasting and evaluation. The team consists of very experienced individuals that 
come from several different countries, i.e. Wales, Germany, India, Denmark and China to name a few. 
The different background gives the team a tremendous advantage in sustaining its position as one of the 
largest single lubricant buyer in the maritime industry. The department’s values are to have a good client 
relationship regarding operational integrity, reliability and effective cost management. The knowledge 
within the department along with the supplier relationships, market intelligence and global purchasing 
volumes helps to obtain the best pricing, quality and service for the clients (Lubrication Oil, n.d.).

4.2 Case Description 
Maersk Oil Trading Lubricants (MOTL) main activities are sourcing lubricants for the Maersk Group 
vessel fleet, i.e. Maersk Line, Maersk Tankers and Maersk Supply Service. This includes both 
lubricating oils and greases. The current setup entails MOTL buying and arranging delivery of oils and 
greases from third party suppliers, but the company has recently identified business opportunities within 
the lubricating greases segment. Currently there are 13 suppliers providing the vessels with 118 different 
types of greases, as the current procurement process in Figure 21 illustrates. MOTL has detected that 
instead of 118 different types of greases the fleet could manage with merely six types.  
 
Currently MOTL is a service provider to the vessel fleet, which is validating orders from vessels and 
consequently purchase lubricants for the customer from the supplier. However, with this new business 
oppertunity, MOTL has identified an opportunity to expand their activities from merely being a service 
provider to being both a service provider and a merchant of the product they are servicing to the vessels. 
As Figure 22 demonstrates, the idea is for MOTL to purchase grease directly from the manufacturer and 
supply their own brand of lubricating grease. Therefore instead of the vessel fleet having 13 different 
suppliers with 118 grease types there would only be one supplier with six grease types. This should 
provide MOTL the possibility to lower the cost its customer is currently spending on grease since the 
suppliers are eliminated. MOTL is certain that reducing the number of greases and suppliers yields lower 
complexity on-board vessels since there would be fewer grease types and all vessels would use the same 
range. Additionally, the plan is to further minimise complexity with a colouring strategy of the grease 
where each grease has its own colour. Each colour is then matched with a machinery on board the 
vessels, which should decrease chances of equipment part being applied with the wrong grease.  
However, MOTL is not certain if this new setup is applicable for their business since it requires a 
configuration of a supply chain, something that MOTL has no experience in developing. Therefore, a 
thorough analysis is performed to understand whether the company should undertake these changes or 
continue their current setup. It should be noted that this opportunity involves only the grease part of the 
lubrication for Maersk Group vessel fleet, not the oil. This means that the suppliers currently used are 
not cut out entirely, only on the purchase of lubricating grease.  The case study of this report will merely 
focus on the vessel fleet within Maersk Line since it is considered a relevant starting point for this new 
project. The conclusions from the study could then be used to expand further to fleets within Maersk 
Tankers and Maersk Supply Service.   
 
 
 
Figure 21: Current procurement process for grease.

4.3 Stakeholder Analysis 
A stakeholder analysis was conducted to identify the main stakeholders at the case company before 
starting the analysis of the project. As Thompson (1967) states, a stakeholder is “any group or individual 
who can affect or is affected by achievement of the organisation’s objectives person, group or 
organisation that has interest or concern in an organization”. For the case project the main stakeholders 
are the customer (Maersk Line), current grease suppliers, grease manufacturer, senior executive at MOT, 
the steering group at MOTL and the employees of the department (Embleton, 2016). The Power/Interest 
grid for stakeholder prioritisation (Mendelow, 1991) is used to analyse the main stakeholders of the case 
company.  
The power/interest is demonstrated on the left in Figure 23, the four quadrants are explained as follows 
(Thompson R. , 2002): 
 
High power, interested people: The people you must fully engage and make the greatest effort to 
satisfy. 
 
High power, less interested people: Put enough work in with these people to keep them satisfied, 
but not so much that they become bored with your message. 
 
Low power, interested people: Keep these people adequately informed, and talk to them to ensure 
that no major issues are arising. These people can often be very helpful with the detail of your 
project. 
 
Low power, less interested people: Monitor these people, but do not bore them with excessive 
communication.

4.4 Summary 
The case company for this project is introduced in this chapter. The company that was chosen to carry 
out the study with is the Lubricant Department within Maersk Oil Trading (MOT). which is  part of the 
Maersk Group, one of the largest container shipping companies in the world.  
MOTL provides a service to the Maersk Group’s vessels that involves purchase of the required lubricant 
oil and greases that is used on board each vessel. After discovering that the Maersk Group’s vessels are 
supplied with 118 different types of grease from 13 suppliers, while the company could manage with 
only six different types, MOTL identified business opportunities in the lubricating grease segment. The 
idea is to cease purchasing grease from the suppliers and purchase the products directly from the 
manufacturer. In the current set up, MOTL functions as a service provider but with the indented future 
set up MOTL would be a merchant of the lubricating grease. This change would require a new structure 
of the supply chain for the grease, which MOTL has no experience in. Therefore, a thorough analysis 
will be performed to understand whether the company should undertake these changes or continue its 
current setup.  The primary requirements for the case are to develop the relevant supply chain strategy 
for MOTL to distribute their own brand of greases to Maersk Line vessels. All findings for the current 
situation of the case company are presented in the following chapter.  
43 
==PAGE:59==
 
Current Situation Study (Findings) 
 
 
 
5. Current Situation Study (Findings) 
This chapter presents the current situation at the company and the information required in order to 
conduct an analysis and present possible scenarios of supply chain strategies for the lubricating greases.  
5.1 The Product 
Lubricating oils and greases are the products that MOTL is merchandising. These products are used for 
various purposes on board vessels, such as lubricating machinery, turn-buckles and twist-locks for 
containers. The reason for lubrication is to prevent wear, reduce friction and to dissipate heat that is 
generated between metal surfaces. Crude oil is an unrefined petroleum product that can be refined to 
manufacture useful products such as gasoline, gas oil and lubricants as Figure 24 illustrates. Lubricant 
oil is made from base oil (70-98%) and additives (2-30%), while lubricating greases are made from base 
oil (75-95%), thickener (5-20%) and additives (0-10%). The additives and thickener are used to enhance 
the performance of the lubricants and the properties of additives and thickeners are chosen based on the 
purpose of use. Both grease and oil are used for lubrication but how they are used is different. Grease is 
thicker than oil and does not run. Oil needs a circulation system while grease stays put and is therefore 
suitable for bearings, pumps, open gear systems and wire ropes (Embleton, 2016). 
 
 
Figure 24: The process from crude oil to useful products (Maersk, 2015) 
 
 
44 
==PAGE:60==
 
Current Situation Study (Findings)

The current number of different grease types used on board vessels of the whole fleet is 118, but within 
Maersk Line it is 83. By introducing the company’s own brand of greases the number of different grease 
types would decrease to six types, with one supplier, i.e. the manufacturer. The new greases and their 
description are in Table 24.

5.1.3 Packaging 
The packaging of the lubricating greases from the manufacturer would be pails and/or drums, where the 
pail of grease is 18 kg. It would be delivered on 1,14 x 1,14 pallets in 20’ containers. In each container 
there can either be 20 pallets of pails and on every pallet there are 30 pails. This means that in each 
container there is space for maximum 600 pails (Colones, 2016), as illustrated in Figure 25. 
 
 
 
 
5.1.4 Chemtura 
As stated before, the idea is to decrease the number of grease suppliers and purchase directly from a 
manufacturer, which is the chemical company Chemtura. Chemtura is a global specialty chemicals 
company with leading positions in diversified markets. It manufactures in 11 countries and sells its 
products in over 80 countries. The company’s headquarters are in Pennsylvania, USA but even though 
its manufacturing takes place in many countries, grease is mainly manufactured in Canada (Chemtura, 
2014). Presently, some of MOTL’s current suppliers buy their greases from the manufacturer Chemtura 
so it is well known that the grease is of acceptable quality. MOTL discussed with several possible 
manufacturing companies but Chemtura was the only one that was flexible enough to accommodate 
MOTL’s requirements for this project and was therefore chosen (Wells, 2016). The manufacturing lead-
time at Chemtura is 30 days.

5.2 Current Procurement Process 
All lubricating oil and grease for Maersk Line vessels are bought through the lubricants department at 
MOT. Each vessel reviews the inventory regularly and orders the lubricant and grease necessary. The 
order process can be seen in Figure 26. The chief engineer on board the vessel puts in a requisition in 
the procurement system for the required products. The procurement team at MOTL reviews the 
requisition and then makes a purchase order to send to the relevant supplier. Normally it is the same 
supplier that supplies the same vessel each time. The main suppliers have a warehouse at the main 
harbours and therefore the lead-time is not long, i.e. approximately 1-3 days (Spanggaard, 2016). 
 
  
Figure 26: Current procurement process. 
5.2.1 Lifting Strategy 
Usually, each vessel has to order new lubricants every six months. To remain competitive MOTL 
forecasts the pricing of lubricants and determines the best point for their clients, e.g. ML, to reorder their 
supplies. This is called the Lifting Strategy. The vessels make an order at a given point and receive their 
order when they reach their designated lifting port, which is the most economical one on their trade 
route. The price forecasting is based on crude oil prices. Changes in the crude oil affect the pricing of 
marine lubricants due to the lubricants deriving from it as a raw material. The process is illustrated in 
Figure 27, showing that if the price of crude oil moves down at one point in time it will hit the pricing 
of lubricant base oil 2-3 months later which will then hit MOTL’s clients 2-3 months later, in total giving 
a delay of 4-6 months. This way MOTL has advance warning of upcoming price changes and can 
forecast in which quarter it is optimal for vessels to order lubricants. For example, in 2015 it was 
estimated that in quarters one and two the price would decrease but not drop further down in quarters 
three and four so the vessels were advised to order lubricants for the whole year in the second quarter. 
Both working capital and change in price need to be considered when deciding how many times per year 
grease should be bought. The way MOTL negotiates with their suppliers is that a base price is made and 
then if the market goes up or down the price is adjusted according to an agreed mechanism (Fierlings, 
2016). The crude oil is sold to the oil refiners who refine the product into useful materials such as 
lubricant base oil. When their storage is empty of the old crude oil they will start replenishing with new 
crude oil and the pricing on that batch of base oil will be based on the crude oil price. This base oil is 
then sold to MOTL’s suppliers, who add additives and formulate the product which is then sold to 
MOTL’s clients. 
Vessel makes a 
requisition
MOTL reviews 
the requistion 
and makes a

Figure 27: The process from drilled crude oil to lubricants aboard the vessels  
5.2.2 Current Financing 
MOTL’s current financial income comes from their customers, i.e. Maersk Line, Maersk Tanker, 
Maersk Supply Service and a few other external customers (see Figure 28). Each year a contract is made 
with each customer where the amount of financing is decided, based on last year’s number of purchase 
orders and the amount of time each one takes. The contract also includes technical support from MOTL. 
Another part of the company’s financing is a R&D relationship that MOTL has established with a 
lubricant additives company where that company pays a contribution in order to share technical expertise 
between the two teams. MOTL also provides the company with access to Maersk’s vessels for testing 
purposes. By having their own brand of greases MOTL gets the opportunity to increase financial income 
and strengthen their financial situation. It is essential to consider that although Maersk Line, Maersk 
Tankers and Maersk Supply Service are a part of Maersk group, they are not obligated to use the 
procurement and technical service offered by MOTL. If these companies are offered an improved 
contract there is nothing preventing them to take their business elsewhere. MOTL’s own brand of 
lubricating grease would help to make the company more independent of the business relationship with 
Maersk Line, Maersk Tankers and Maersk Supply Service, since it opens the possibility of 
merchandising its product to a broader customer base. It therefore also provides some extra financial 
security should one of these contracts cease to exist for some reason.

5.3 Maersk Line Vessel Schedule 
Maersk Line consists of 235 vessels, which are located all around the world. The vessel routes can be 
compared to bus schedules. There is a predefined schedule throughout the year for all vessels and each 
vessel has a different schedule.  Therefore, it is easy to know the location of each vessel at a given time. 
The routes are divided up to eight different areas; East-west network, Africa, Central America, Intra-
Asia, Intra- Europe, Oceania, South America and Middle East & Indian subcontinent. All vessels require 
lubricating grease for their engines and other equipment and therefore it is necessary to structure a supply 
chain that can deliver grease to all vessels regardless of their position. The vessel routes, the different 
locations/harbours for vessels, offices and local bases of operations are shown in the world map in Figure 
29. 
 
Figure 29: The different locations of harbours and local offices for Maersk Line around the world 
(The Maersk Group, n.d.)

5.5 Warehouse Location 
As mentioned in Section 5.3 Maersk Line vessels follow a schedule and, the vessels are located all 
around the world. The main harbours that Maersk Line vessels stop at are shown in Figure 30. The 
harbours are arranged in descending order, i.e. from the harbour lifting the highest amount of lubricants 
on board vessels to the lowest amount. As Figure 30 shows, Singapore and Rotterdam are the two 
dominating harbours. According to Wells (2016) it is highly unlikely that these harbours will be removed 
from the vessels schedule and there is easy excess to these harbours. From this information Singapore 
and Rotterdam are used as main harbours for this case study, for instance when looking into warehouse 
locations.

5.6  Consignment Shipment 
A consignment shipment is delivered on a quarterly basis to all vessels at Maersk Line. This shipment 
includes spare parts required on board each vessel. Currently MOTL includes one grease type in these 
container shipments, which is used for the turnbuckles and twist locks on the containers. This same 
grease is used for all the vessels. The grease and the spare parts are stored in a warehouse in Rotterdam, 
which belongs to a company named Estron. This warehouse is used by Group Procurement, which is a 
department within Maersk Group. Maersk is Estron’s biggest customer, 80% of the products that are 
stored in the warehouse belong to Maersk (Koning, 2016). Group procurement is in charge of the 
shipments and organises them. MOTL has not been obligated to pay additional fees for storing the grease 
in the warehouse or having the grease as part of the consignment shipment which is delivered to all 
Maersk Line vessels (Spanggaard, 2016). The price for the consignment is shown in Appendix A. 
5.7  Delivery Options 
There are several different delivery options that will be investigated. The manufacturer Chemtura is 
located in West Hill, Canada and the lubricating grease needs to be delivered on board every vessel of 
Maersk Line. MOTL could arrange its shipping and have its own warehouse in key locations, e.g. 
Rotterdam and Singapore. Another option could be to get Chemtura to organize the delivery and have 
it included as part of their price; they have warehouses in for example Rotterdam and Singapore. Another 
option is to have the greases included as part of the consignment shipments, explained in Section 5.6, 
then the products would be stored in a warehouse owned by the company Estron. Possible delivery 
companies are investigated in the analysis in Chapter 6. 
5.8 Transport Options 
Different transport options need to be considered regarding delivering the grease to all vessels. In Figure 
31 the manufacturing and possible warehouse locations are marked on a world map. As can be seen, 
different types of transport methods need to be established, for example by sea and road. In Section 5.3 
the Maersk line vessel schedule is presented and it can be seen that not all vessels stop in Rotterdam or 
Singapore during their scheduled route, which means that other options must to be established for those 
vessels. One possibility is to have the grease included as part of the consignment shipment which was 
described in Section 5.6 for the vessels that do not stop in Rotterdam or Singapore. For the vessels that 
51 
==PAGE:67==
 
Current Situation Study (Findings) 
 
 
 
do stop in one of these places the possible warehouses there can be used. These possibilities will be 
analysed further in Chapter 6. 
 
 
Figure 31: Map with information of manufacturing locations and possible warehouses. 
5.9 Grease Cost and other Cost Factors 
The grease cost differs if bought through a third party instead of buying it straight from a manufacturer 
such as Chemtura. In the next two chapters a price comparison is made between buying grease through 
a third party or buying it straight from the manufacturer. It should be considered that the price listed in 
the current grease cost includes the delivery and warehousing cost but the price for buying straight from 
manufacturer only includes the cost for manufacturing the product. However, by having this data it is 
possible to see how much margin there is leftover which can be spent on delivery and warehousing. 
5.9.1  Current Grease Cost 
Both the amount of grease ordered and the price varies between the years 2010 – 2015, as can be seen 
in Table 30. This is because new contracts are made approximately every two years, which affects the 
price, and the lifting strategy explained in Section 5.2.1 affects both the amount ordered and the price. 
The numbers are gathered from the spend data of MOTL.

5.10 Summary 
This chapter covers the current situation at the company, the current procurement process, product 
demand and cost. It gives an introduction to the manufacturer and the product, lubricating grease, and 
the product portfolio. It also introduces possible warehouses and transportation options and prices.  
The chapter starts with an introduction of the product, grease and the packaging. The manufacturer is 
introduced along with the product portfolio. The current financing of the lubricants department at MOT 
is explained and the demand for the product is demonstrated. Maersk Line, one of the main customers 
of MOTL, is introduced as wells as the location of its vessels. It is established that Rotterdam and 
Singapore are the two main harbours used in the analysis in Chapter 6. The possible warehouse locations 
and the warehouse requirements are stated along with different transportation options. The last part of 
the chapter examines the cost factors and they are described, that is, the grease cost, inventory cost and 
the transportation cost. When building up the different scenarios these factors can determine which 
scenario the company will choose. The current findings are analysed further in Chapter 6.  
54 
==PAGE:70==
 
Analysis 
 
 
 
6. Analysis 
This chapter highlights the analysis conducted for this case study, which is based on the quantitative and 
qualitative data gathered from the literature review in Chapter 2 and the current situation study in 
Chapter 5. The chapter covers the whole process of configuring a new supply chain and proposes options 
for the scenario building in Chapter 7. The analysis focuses on the maritime industry with Maersk Oil 
Trading Lubricants as a focal point.  
6.1 Analysing the Supply Chain  
In order to suggest the relevant supply chain strategy for Maersk Oil Trading Lubricants an analysis of 
different factors discussed in the literature review are in order. As Fisher’s model showed, in Section 0, 
the supply chain strategy and the type of product should be aligned and since the product is a known 
factor of the case company, it is used as a starting point of this analysis.  
6.1.1 Analysing the Product 
As Fisher’s matrix indicates, the supply chain should be chosen from the product’s nature, where 
products are categorized into two types: functional products and innovative products. A starting point 
in the analysis of the relevant supply chain strategy for MOTL is analysing the product by examining 
the first two characteristics in Table 34, since they are considered the most distinguishing factors 
between functional and innovative products. 
Table 34: The characteristics of the two product types identified by Fisher (1997). 
 
Functional Products 
Innovative Products 
Characteristics 
 
Steady demand 
 
Long lifecycles (>2 years)  
 
Low profit margins (5-20%) 
 
Satisfy basic needs 
 
Price sensitive 
 
Unsteady demand 
 
Short lifecycles (3 months – 1 year) 
 
High profit margins (20-60%) 
 
Constantly changing 
 
High product variety 
 
Product Demand 
The demand for the new grease is estimated from the amount of grease used on board all vessels in the 
last 6 years, i.e. 2010-2015.  As Table 25 in Section 5.1.2 indicates, the average demand varies between 
each year. The reason for this change in demand is due to the lifting strategy explained in Section 5.2.1. 
The order of the current lubricant grease is dependent on the price of crude oil, which results in unsteady 
demand. For example, if the crude oil price is low and MOTL forecasts that it will not decrease further 
the vessels are advised to purchase the grease in the optimal quadrant of the year. This means that all 
the grease could be purchased twice in one year then once in the next. However, in the contract between 
MOTL and Chemtura the lubricant grease price is not dependant on the crude oil price and therefore the 
grease is purchased with an even interval each year, e.g. every three months for each vessel. It is 
therefore considered that from each vessel comes a steady demand, which classifies the product as 
functional, according to Fisher. 
 
Product Life Cycle 
A product life cycle is defined as the time a product is viable in the marketplace (Kokemuller, n.d.). 
Grease in general has existed for centuries, and has evolved over time, i.e. the grease used 100 years ago 
is not the same grease used today, though it serves the same purpose (Embleton, 2016). The usage for 
grease is important in the maritime industry and therefore it is highly unlikely that grease in general will 
be substituted with another product. The grease is dependent on the equipment used on board the vessels, 
so as long as the equipment stays the same the demand for lubricant grease stays unchanged. Moreover, 
a base trial was conducted on one of the vessels and showed good results. From this information and the 
55 
==PAGE:71==
 
Analysis 
 
 
 
fact that a lubricant grease is a mature product the lubricant grease for MOTL is considered to have a 
long product life cycle, i.e. more than two years. The lifetime of a grease is approximately four years 
(Embleton, 2016).  
The product is considered satisfy basic needs and is price sensitive, meaning that the customer could 
easily turn to the competitor if the sales price is high. The profit margin can however not be calculated 
at this point since MOTL has not decided on a sales price for the product. Nevertheless, these factors 
indicate that the new lubricant grease of MOTL should be categorized as a functional product. 
6.1.2 Analysing the Supply Chain Strategy 
According to Fisher’s matrix the relevant supply chain strategy for MOTL, as it has a functional product, 
is to have it physically efficient. With an efficient supply chain the main focus is to supply the predictable 
demand efficiently, at the lowest cost possible without reducing quality. The following points are used 
as a guideline: 
 
Information sharing: Good communication platform/setup between each stage of the supply chain 
 
Inventory strategy: Generate high returns and minimum inventory in each stage of the chain.  
 
Lead-time focus: Shorten lead-times without increased cost.  
 
Choosing suppliers: Select mostly for quality and cost.  
 
In Figure 32 the basic structure of the future supply chain for MOTL is shown. The manufacturer is 
considered a supplier since MOTL purchases the product straight from Chemtura, but since the choice 
of manufacturer has already been made the selection of the manufacturer is out of scope. Currently the 
supply chain is not configured, so the aim is to investigate transportation and warehouses with the 
objective to minimise cost without reducing quality. Therefore, to comprehend the different options in 
the configuration structure, the option of outsourcing is considered.  
 
 
 
 
Figure 32: Basic structure of the suggested future supply chain. 
6.1.3 Outsourcing or Insourcing  
Generally, outsourcing gives companies the chance to focus on their core competences (Sislian & Satir, 
2000). As Chorafas (2003) demonstrated, the core function of a company should not be outsourced and 
therefore it is concluded that MOTL should maintain the service they provide to Maersk Line, i.e. their 
specialised knowledge on lubricants, insourced. The option of outsourcing is however considered to see 
if there is a possibility to outsource other activities without bad impact on the core competences. There 
are two main things that can be outsourced in the supply chain for the new product at MOTL, i.e. 
transportation and storage facilities 
6.1.3.1 Transportation Outsourcing 
One of the main reasons companies outsource their logistics is low cost. That is, by leveraging through 
economy of scale and low rates in transportation, which has increased with the growing competence in 
the transportation market (Bernstein, 2001). Moreover, companies that specialize in transportation are 
more likely to be more efficient than companies that are not specialised (Kruse, 2002). 
56 
==PAGE:72==
 
Analysis 
 
 
 
Orgorelc (2007) discussed a framework for logistics outsourcing decisions, i.e. transportation, where 
the decision for outsourcing is based on two considerations; how critical the logistics performance is in 
the market in question and if logistics are a core competency of the company. As earlier mentioned, the 
core competency of MOTL is offering lower cost of their product and their strong knowledge of its 
function and therefore if the framework in Figure 33 is considered for MOTL the transportation factor 
of the supply chain is not a core competency in the business but it is considered a critical success factor. 
The reason for this is that the lubricant grease is essential for the vessels to function so the delivery of 
the product is crucial, i.e. if there are constant delays the customer could turn to the competitor. 
However, as the framework outlines, the transportation could be outsourced but at the same time the 
process would be controlled by MOTL. For example, if something is miscalculated and a prompt order 
is required, MOTL is responsible for contacting the delivery company and monitors that the order is 
delivered.  
6.1.3.2 Warehouse Outsourcing 
The logistics framework is used as a guideline in order to determine the decision on outsourcing the 
storage of the product. The warehousing is important as well as transportation of the product and is 
therefore considered a critical success factor in the market, since the warehouses increases the 
availability of the product for the customer. However, the warehouse is not considered core competency 
in the business. From these conclusions the framework is applied and results in the same quadrant as 
logistics, as Figure 34 illustrates. As O’Byrne identified (2015), outsourcing the storage of the product 
will result in a service with labour force specialised in warehouse functions, given that the service 
provider is reliable, a reduction in capital expenditure, since the company is free from property leases, 
labour & equipment cost, and the risk associated with managing labour & processes shifts to the 3PL 
service provider, even during peaks in demand.  
Figure 33: Framework for logistics outsourcing decisions (Ogorelc, 2007). 
57 
==PAGE:73==
 
Analysis 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
It should also be considered that the amount of grease consumed each year is merely around 10 
containers for Maersk Line, so handling transportation and setting up a warehouse is a lot of effort for 
such a small amount of grease. In order to stay competitive the product should have a low sales price, 
and therefore the low cost of all activities related to the product are in order. If the warehousing or 
transportation services are poor, e.g. frequent errors or delays, it could result in higher cost for MOTL 
and as a consequence the customer could turn to the competitor. Therefore, it is considered that both 
factors are critical success factors but at the same time not core competences, since these factors are not 
the ones that keep MOTL competitive, but monitoring them is an essential link in the supply chain in 
order to stay competitive.  
6.1.4 Different Outsourcing Options 
In order to obtain an efficient supply chain the outsourced activities should also be included and 
therefore the selection of the suppliers is crucial. According to Fisher (1997), the suppliers should be 
chosen on the base of quality and cost. It is therefore necessary to identify which performances are the 
most relevant for each supplier and which target value should be set in order to achieve an optimal 
solution. In Section 2.5.3.1 the five stages of the supplier selection process are identified. In the 
following sections the first three stages are addressed, highlighted in Figure 35 and the last two are 
discussed in the recommendation (Chapter 7).

6.1.5 Analysing the End User 
The next step is to analyse the end user of the supply chain and see if the fact that the customer is moving 
affects other stages, i.e. if there is need for any degree of responsiveness. As stated before the demand 
is steady but the behaviour of the customer is different from other industries, such as fashion and food 
industry. As discussed in Chapter 2, there is a limited amount of literature regarding end-users of supply 
chains. However, in this analyses customers are divided into two groups from the type of purchase 
behaviour: one were the customer purchases the product from a store and another where the customer 
purchases a product online, see Table 39. If a product is purchased from a store, the customer locates 
the store without placing an order and buys a product. The store/product has a fixed location and the 
purchaser is a moving unit, i.e. the customer tails the product. If a product is purchased online, the 
customer provides his address (or the nearest post office) where the product will be delivered. The 
purchaser has a fixed location and the product is a moving unit, i.e. the product tails the customer. The 
end user in the maritime industry is however considered a mixture of both, i.e. vessels are the moving 
unit but the product is as well, since the product tails the customer but the customer does not have a 
fixed location. 
Table 39: Customer behaviour. 
Product purchased 
Customer 
Merchant 
Behaviour 
From a store 
Moving unit 
Fixed location 
Customer tails the product 
Online 
Fixed location 
Moving unit 
Product tails the customer 
For a vessel 
Moving unit 
Moving unit 
Product tails the customer 
 
To further explain, examples of stock-out scenarios in the fashion, food and pharmaceutical industries 
are considered, see Table 40. In the food industry it is most likely that the customer will turn to the 
competitor, while in the fashion industry the customer will perhaps wait until the specific clothes are in 
stock again. However, in the pharmaceutical industry, assuming that the medicine is crucial for the 
customer, the customer would most likely need to tail the product elsewhere. A stock-out in the maritime 
industry is however considered more difficult, i.e. for the vessel to cope in these situations. This is due 
to two factors: it is not as easy for the vessel to turn to the competitor as it is in the food industry for 
instance, and the crucial products are necessary for the vessel to function. The vessel is often far from 
the next harbour where the product could be delivered and this could result in an emergency delivery. 
Therefore, it is essential for the vessels to receive their products on time, especially crucial products for 
their equipment, for example. Moreover, many stores provide online shopping but in many cases this 
61 
==PAGE:77==
 
Analysis 
 
 
 
alternative is only available in selected countries. For the countries where online shopping is not 
available there is no option for a potential customer to receive a product, due to location. In the maritime 
industry however, this is not an option. If a vessel does not stop at a harbour near the warehouse with 
the product in question, the merchant is required to deliver the product to the vessel, independent on the 
vessel’s location. 
Table 40: Possible stock-out scenarios. 
Industry 
Product crucial 
In case of stock-out 
Food 
No 
Customer turns to competitor 
Fashion 
No 
Customer waits for product 
Pharmaceutical 
Yes 
Customer locates the product elsewhere 
Maritime 
Yes 
Emergency delivery 
 
In this case study Maersk Line is the end-user and as stated before, Maersk Line has a predefined 
schedule where both time and location is known. Therefore, the warehouses can be located in the best 
place possible in order to service all vessels. The warehouses can in this case be viewed as stores where 
the customer tails the product. The vessels that do not stop where the warehouses are located is viewed 
as the online purchaser, where the merchant knows the location of the customer and delivers the product. 
If a delay occurs in the vessel’s schedule, i.e. +/- some hours or even days in some ports, the delivery 
company is in close contact with the vessel agent so the delivery company can estimate when the vessel 
arrives, meaning that it should not entail an emergency delivery.  
As stated in Chapter 5 there are two harbours that will be viewed as main harbours, i.e. Rotterdam and 
Singapore. Considering this setup in terms of the end-user, it was believed that because the destination 
point is moving it requires some degree of responsiveness in the supply chain, perhaps in higher 
inventory level. This contravenes efficient strategy, so the possibility of a hybrid strategy was 
considered. However, since Maersk Line has a predefined schedule and the demand is steady for each 
vessel, it is believed that in this case study the moving destination point does not require a responsive 
supply chain and therefore efficiency should be their focal point. For other cases, such as Maersk 
Tankers and Supply Service where the schedule is unknown, there is a possibility that the supply chain 
requires responsiveness. In these cases perhaps the inventory level should be higher at both warehouse 
locations, since the unknown location of the vessels indicates an unsteady demand, assuming that the 
warehouse closer to the vessel services the vessel. However, these considerations are out of scope in this 
project.  
6.1.6 Business Processes and Customer Order Decoupling Point 
In the literature review three business processes were discussed, i.e. Make-To-Order, Assembly-To-
Order and Make-To-Stock. The long lead-time from an order is placed until delivered to warehouse 
indicates that the MTO option is impractical and a postponement of the customisation of the product is 
impossible since the whole production process of the grease has to be performed in the same mixture at 
the manufacturer. These factors and indications from the literature, i.e. that these strategies are more 
relevant for customised products with a variable demand, indicates that both MTO and ATO are 
irrelevant in this case study. According to the literature, standard products with a steady demand should 
be made to stock where there is relatively easy to manage their inventory, assuming the forecasting is 
right. This is therefore believed to be the appropriate strategy for this case study.  
 
From this conclusion the customer order decoupling point is placed as Figure 36 shows and entails a 
push system. The decoupling point is the last point of the supply chain where inventory is held. The 
figure illustrates how the product is pushed to the warehouses, where the customer then pulls the product. 
However, this is considered a push-system since the product is manufactured based on forecasted 
demand, not the order from the customer, i.e. the order does not entail a change in inventory decisions.  
 
62 
==PAGE:78==
 
Analysis 
 
 
 
 
Figure 36: The location of the customer order decoupling point in the suggested future supply chain. 
6.2 Analysis of Harbour Location  
As Section 5.5 demonstrates, the harbours with the highest delivery rate in terms of oil and grease are 
Singapore and Rotterdam. These harbours will be used as main harbours in this analysis for three main 
reasons: they are unlikely to be removed from vessel routes, there is easy excess to these harbours and 
they are in different continents, which is believed to be essential in order to comprehend the delivery 
area in question, i.e. the whole world. In Section 5.6 a consignment shipment was discussed. This 
shipment can be used for vessels that do not sail to Singapore or Rotterdam. However, if the consignment 
shipment is not an option other measure need to be made, such as analysing each vessel schedule, finding 
optimal delivery location for an order and the relevant cost. All these factors are included in the 
following section. 
6.2.1 Analysing the Vessel Schedule  
The Maersk Line vessel schedule from October 2015 to May 2016 was reviewed. The schedule includes 
235 vessels and 238 harbours. Each vessel has its own route and the number of harbours in each schedule 
varies between vessels.  Rotterdam and Singapore are used as main harbours and from that the harbours 
could be divided as Table 41 demonstrates.  
Table 41: Vessels arriving at Singapore and Rotterdam.

Each harbour was arranged on the base of its distance from Rotterdam and Singapore, respectively. See 
Table 86 in Appendix F. The task was to assign a harbour to each vessel where that vessel would then 
receive its order. In order to simplify the calculations, distances were divided into six zones represented 
by different colours, as Table 43 shows. The difference between Singapore and Rotterdam in scaling of 
distances was adjusted in order to achieve six zones for both groups. An example of how the harbours 
were arranged is shown in Table 44, where the harbours are arranged in a descending order based on the 
number of vessels arriving, and the colour represents the distance from a harbour.  
To achieve a broader perspective the schedule was divided into three scenarios; A, B and C, as Figure 
37 illustrates. In scenario A all vessels arriving at Rotterdam are excluded, in scenario B vessels arriving 
at Singapore are excluded and in scenario C all vessels arriving to either Rotterdam or Singapore are

excluded, since the goal is to find harbour locations for vessels not arriving at the main harbours. As

system.

Each scenario was further divided into two different scenarios in the selection of a harbour for the vessel 
in question: 
I. 
Distance from the main harbour is dominant, i.e. if a vessel goes to x different harbours the 
harbour that is closest to the main harbour in question is selected for that vessel.  
II. 
A harbour is selected in terms of both the number of vessels arriving and the distance from main 
harbour, e.g. if a harbour x has 30 vessels arriving and harbour y has 25, but y is located closer 
to e.g. Rotterdam then harbour y is chosen, assuming that x and y are in the same distance zone. 
Here harbours with >20 vessels arriving were focused on. 
An example for both executions, by using Table 44 and Rotterdam as the main harbour, would be:  
I. 
Firstly, Vostochniy Port is selected for all vessels arriving there and Salalah is chosen secondly. 
Both of these harbours are in zone 2. The next harbour would be Manzillo in zone 3, since of 
all harbours in zone 3 (in this example) Manzillo is closest to Rotterdam. This is performed until 
all vessels have been allocated a harbour. See distances in Table 86 in Appendix F. 
II. 
Here the same harbours are chosen in the first two selections, i.e. Vostochniy Port and Salalah. 
However, in the third selection the harbour that has the most no. of vessels arriving of all 
harbours in zone 3 (in this example) is selected, i.e. Busan.  This is performed until all vessels 
have been allocated a harbour.

6.2.2 Transportation Cost to Harbours 
The results from Section 6.2.1 provide five different scenarios of optimal harbour locations for order 
delivery to each vessel.  As stated in Chapter 3, the distance for each harbour was found by using Google 
maps and from that divided into six zones to simplify and provide a greater guideline in the selection of 
harbours. However, the delivery cost is evaluated on the distance from main harbours in each scenario 
to the selected harbours, i.e. the cost is not the same within each zone. The cost for each distance is 
based on prices from the chosen delivery company and the calculations are shown in Appendix G. 
The total cost for each scenario was calculated by using the last two points of assumptions in Table 45, 
the results from the harbour locations analysis and the cost from Table 82 in Appendix F, by using 
equation 6 The results for each scenario, including harbour locations, number of vessels arriving and 
cost for delivery to these harbours is shown in Table 83, Table 84 and Table 85 in Appendix F. A 
summary of these results is listed is Table 46 where the total cost presented is the cost for delivering 
orders to the vessels not arriving at the main harbour.  The total cost represents the delivery cost for 
three months of supply, since each vessel receives an order quarterly each year. The table indicates that 
scenario C is the most viable choice, showing that spreading the warehouses enables lower cost in 
delivery to vessels. This is believed to be for two reasons: more vessels are arriving at the warehouse 
locations since now there are two warehouses in question, and it is easier to maintain delivery distances 
at a minimum since there are two warehouses to send orders from, and therefore lower delivery cost.  
The difference in scenario 1 and 2 is that scenario 2 results in fewer harbours and therefore more volume 
discounts, i.e. more than 15 vessels arriving at the same harbour. However, this factor does not seem to 
be dominant since scenario 2 results in higher cost in both scenario A and B. An additional factor to 
consider is that at any time in these three months the vessels could place an order, which means that 
gathering many orders in one delivery is perhaps not feasible or easy to achieve. It is therefore concluded 
that scenario 2 is not a viable option. The reason for the cost difference between scenarios A, B and C 
however is considered to be connected to the difference in vessels that the main harbour needs to service.  
The three scenarios that are selected to use in the scenario building in Chapter 7 are scenarios A1, B1 
and C.

6.3 Inventory Management 
Managing inventory is essentially about balancing supply and demand. Companies search for a strategy 
to help balance between the working capital in inventories and service level (Richards, 2011). Other 
reasons for holding inventory are predictability, fluctuations in demand, unreliability of supply, price 
protection, quantity discounts and lower ordering cost (Muller, 2003).  In this section the analysis of the 
appropriate service level, the reorder point, safety stock and the optimal order size are recommended for 
the case company. These factors are all important in order to stay inside the optimal range of the 
inventory level, illustrated in Figure 38. 
 
 
Figure 38: The optimal range of inventory and the danger (SAPICS, 2015). 
6.3.1 Service Level 
As stated in Section 2.7.3 a service level is one of the most important factors when managing inventories 
(Gunasekaran & Tirtiroglu, 2001). However, the exact service level cannot be calculated, it has to be 
decided in regards to the customer service that the company is willing to offer (Simchi-Levi, Simchi-
Levi, & Kaminsky, 2008). As Figure 39 illustrates, increasing the service level gives an exponential 
increase in safety stock required, i.e. increase in safety stock is non-linear (Scott, Lundgren, & 
Thompson, 2011). A change from 95% service level to 98% service level does not result in only 3% 
increase in safety stock but significantly more. This trade-off between service level and inventory should 
be considered in order to state a realistic service level.  
The recommended service level for MOTL is 98% since a stock-out could yield severe affects. A higher 
service level is not chosen since with the new grease range all the vessels would use the same grease 
and could therefore in worst case scenario loan products from each other.

6.3.2 Safety Stock 
As stated in Section 2.7.3 the safety stock must cover the demand variability during lead time. In the 
section, two equations were provided to calculate the safety stock but equation (7) is used for the case 
company since the lead time is the primary concern and the demand is relatively stable. The variables 
used in the safety stock equation are listed in Table 47.

The safety stock is calculated based on the scenarios presented in Table 42 in Section 6.2.1. In Scenario 
A all inventory is stored in Rotterdam, Scenario B all inventory is stored in Singapore and Scenario C 
all inventory is stored both in a warehouse in Rotterdam and in Singapore. The inventory is divided 
between warehouses based on the number of vessels arriving to each harbour (inside zone 1) and vessels 
that need delivery outside zone 1 as Table 42 shows. For the vessels outside zone 1 either the 
consignment shipment or transportation company U could be used for delivery. Therefore each scenario 
is further divided into these two options. However, since the warehouse for the consignment shipment 
is located in Rotterdam only scenario B and C are split up, with or without consignment inventory. 
The total required safety stock for each scenario was calculated and the results are listed in Table 
48showing the total amount of the required safety stock in each location. As the table shows there are 
22 pallets of safety stock in scenario B and 18 in scenario A. The reason for this is the difference in lead 
time to each harbour from the manufacturer as shown in Table 47. In scenario B-consignment all the 
inventory is stored in Singapore for the vessels arriving inside zone 1 but for the vessels arriving outside 
zone 1 the consignment warehouse in Rotterdam is used. The lead time for the consignment shipment 
is the same as for Rotterdam, i.e. 60 days. Scenario C – consignment is the same as B – consignment 
but the inventory in Rotterdam is both for consignment shipment and for vessels outside zone 1.  
The result from the safety stock calculation show that Scenario B has the highest amount of required 
safety stock since all inventory is stored in Singapore with a total lead time of 90 days. The safety stock 
for each product within all the scenarios is shown in Appendix C and Appendix I.

6.3.3 Reorder Point 
As stated in Section 2.7.2 the Reorder Point (ROP) is the point when a company should make a purchase 
order. It is traditionally the lead time demand plus the safety stock, see equation 8, where the lead time 
demand is the consumption of the product during lead time.  
 
 
The Reorder Point was calculated for the five different scenarios mentioned in Section 6.3.2. In Table 
49 the result from the calculations are demonstrated. This table only shows a comparison for the ROP 
between the different scenarios but not for every product within each scenario. The reorder point for all 
products within each scenario are shown in Table 74 -Table 78 in Appendix D and Appendix I. The 
ROP will be used in the following section as a comparison on the interval on how often an order should 
be made throughout the year and how high each order size should be.

6.3.4 Optimal Order Size 
As stated in Chapter 2.7.1, the EOQ formula can help decide on the amount of products to purchase 
each time an order is made. With the formula a business can determine the optimal quantity order which 
minimises the total holding and ordering cost (Harris, 1913). In this project the data for ordering cost is 
not provided, hence the equation cannot be used since the result from the formula is highly dependent 
on the order cost. The optimal order size is therefore determined by comparing inventory cost and the 
transportation cost of delivering the product to the warehouse, i.e. which cost factor is more dominant 
over time. The analysis is made for ordering every month, every two months, quarterly, twice a year and 
yearly. The analysed scenarios are the same as presented in Section 6.3.2. 
It should be noted that this analysis only includes the warehouse and transportation options where the 
inventory is warehouse managed. This means that the warehouses with Vendor Managed Inventory are 
not used in this analysis since that it is not applicable. Constraints and assumptions made in the analysis 
are shown in Table 50.

Figure 40 illustrates the cost of purchasing the amount of demanded grease in one month, twelve times 
a year, the demand in two months six times a year, the demand in three months quarterly, the demand 
in six months twice a year or the total demand yearly.  This is done to see how the warehouse price and 
transportation price effect one another. As Figure 40 illustrates it is cheapest to buy and store demand 
for two months. However, when comparing the reorder point from scenario A in Table 51 and the total 
inventory for two months it can be seen that they are very close, since the inventory for two months is 
58 pallets and the reorder point 50 pallets. Since the orders that are made from the vessels are placed 
approximately every three months and not possible to know when a vessel places an order during that 
time, an inventory of only two months can result in stockout. Therefore, the optimal order quantity is 
considered to be three months of demand purchased quarterly.  
If the reorder point and the order frequency on a quarterly basis are compared (see Table 51) it can be 
seen that the inventory would always be slightly higher than the reorder point since the inventory is 78 
pallets but the reorder point 50 pallets. The price difference between inventory of two months and three 
months is $1.000 to $3.000 in all scenarios per year, excluding the safety stock. This is considered to be 
relatively low does therefore not affect the decision on ordering inventory on a quarterly basis. All 
calculations and prices are shown in Appendix E.

6.4 Complexity Management  
The three dimensions that form the complexity cube discussed in Section 2.6, i.e. product, process and 
organisations, should be considered in this future supply chain strategy (Wilson & Perumal, 2010). By 
optimising what is offered to the customer, the new interface and nodes of interaction can lead to 
unnecessary activities. In order to prevent this from happening, the benefits and costs as well as 
operational processes & organisational structure and the supporting IT system, have to be carefully 
managed. In order to do so the process has to be analysed in terms of complexity factors to understand 
what could yield in hidden cost or hidden complexity (Wilson & Perumal, 2010).  
The current situation on board the vessels is explained as unorganised with each vessel using 
approximately 10 different grease products on various equipment. The lubricating grease is a price 
sensitive product, which entails regular change in suppliers due to market competition. This indicates 
that each time a supplier is changed, new products are brought to the vessels and sometimes leaving 
leftovers of old products on board, which increases the chances of incorrect grease being applied to the 
equipment in question. MOTL manufacturing the greases, reducing variety of products and eliminating 
the suppliers suggests a reduction in complexity on board the vessels. Now the employees would merely 
apply six different greases on the equipment and less supplier turnovers, since there is room for MOTL 
to lower the sales prices. However, the effect this has on the complexity in processes and the organisation 
should also be considered, as Wilson & Perumal (2010) demonstrated. Each order in the current setup 
starts with MOTL receiving a request for an order from a Maersk Line vessel. An MOTL employee 
reads over the order, validates it and contacts the relevant suppliers. Each supplier has a set of vessels 
to supply and each vessel has 2-3 suppliers. The new setup has essentially the same procedure. However, 
instead of having two or three grease suppliers, the vessel only has one, but the MOTL employee has to 
contact the relevant warehouse and the delivery company. The warehouse prepares the order and the 
order is delivered either to the harbour near the warehouse or a harbour where the vessel is scheduled. 
The new setup indicates more complexity in some areas since MOTL goes from solely being a service 
provider to a merchant and a service provider. For instance, by eliminating the suppliers the process of 
handling the order essentially stays the same, but with MOTL contacting different collaborators. 
However, this entails a new segment of inventory management where MOTL is responsible for the level 
of inventory in the warehouses and keeping close contact with warehouse managers. This entails 
inventory cost, possible cost in stock-outs and excess inventory as well as possible extra labour. These 
are all factors that need to be considered. However, this change is considered value adding for the 
customer and it enables more transparency through the supply chain. Therefore the increased complexity 
for MOTL is not negative, as long as these factors are closely monitored. Moreover, the organisation 
71 
==PAGE:87==
 
Analysis 
 
 
 
itself is relatively small, has no assets or facilities, which indicates small complexity. A summary of the 
new setup is illustrated in Table 52.

6.5 Risk Analysis  
As discussed in Section 2.5.2 in order to identify uncertainties it is necessary to identify sources of risk 
and the impacts they could have. Although it is impossible to completely eliminate risks from a supply 
chain, it can be reduced. Known risks are identifiable in advance of an occurrence and generally can be 
prevented with risk mitigation efforts. To eliminate risk, a supply chain risk manager must discover the 
cause of that risk, but finding the cause is usually a difficult task and there is no one-size fits all approach 
(APICS Supply Chain Council, 2005). It should be noted that even with easily measurable risk data, 
supply chain complexity makes it a challenge to gather a complete supply chain’s total risk profile 
(APICS Supply Chain Council, 2005).  
There are six steps in managing the supply chain risk process as demonstrated in Figure 12Error! 
Reference source not found. in Section 2.5.2 to establish context, identify risk, assess risk, evaluate 
risk, mitigate risk and monitor risk. In the first step the objective and scope is defined, both internal and 
external. The objective of the supply chain is to minimise cost while maximising the service level for 
customers. In step two, identify risk, the potential risk events that may impact the organisation from 
meeting its goals are collected and documented. The risk management list was conducted as 
demonstrated in Table 53. After establishing the list each risk is classified based on the probability and 
consequences. This was performed with the matrix in Figure 41 as a guideline. 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 41: Classification matrix of probability and consequences. 
High/Low
High/High
Low/Low
High/Low
Propability of occurence 
Severity of consequences 
72 
==PAGE:88==
 
Analysis 
 
 
 
In step three the risk is quantified, (1) for low, (2) for medium and (3) for high, as regards both 
probability of occurrence and the consequence of risk occurrence. These two factors are multiplied 
together which results in a total severity point value, demonstrated in Table 53. The required information 
for quantifying these factors was obtained from five meetings with three team members of MOTL (head 
of MOTL, a daily manager and a lubricant specialist). As Table 53 demonstrates the highest risk is if an 
equipment part is relubricated with wrong grease. As mentioned in Section 6.3.4 the complexity in the 
inventory on-board vessels can be high. The probability of applying the wrong grease is therefore 
quantified as medium, but the consequences high since it could result in equipment failure. The total 
severity number is therefore six.
