The last step in managing risk is to monitor the risk. Improved risk management enables an organisation 
to take a market share from its competitors when a mutual risk strikes which leads to improvements in 
discovering, preventing and addressing smaller risks, which may cost efforts, expense or time. A Supply 
Chain practising risk management is faster to spot risks, faster to respond to it and faster to claim 
advantages from these capabilities. As supply chains become more complex, risk occurrence becomes 
more costly and the importance of risk management increases (Council, 2015). 
6.5.1 Risk Analysis of Warehousing and Transportation 
In Sections 6.1.3.1 and 6.1.3.2 the warehouse and transportation options are analysed. When choosing 
the appropriate option for the supply chain the risk must be considered. A low cost option is always 
attracting for managers to choose but if the risk which might follow that choice is too high the supply 
chain may suffer.  
The warehouse and transportation options are twofold: either the manufacturer is in charge of 
transporting and storing the product, or a third party company is used. If Chemtura is in charge means 
that a Vendor-Managed Inventory is used. Then the supplier has the control and has to make sure that 
the warehouse is stocked and the product is transported on time. If the third party is used, MOTL is in 
charge of monitoring the order processes for warehouses and is in charge of the deliveries. The risks 
which a Vendor Managed Inventory systems can include are (Vendor, 2016):  
 
Size of customer: If the customer is considerably small, the supplier may be less willing to risk 
the financial cost against the probability of receiving a payment. 
 
Trust: Will the supplier deliver as appropriate and will customer consume as expected. 
 
Dependency: VMI is heavily dependent on technology and can be costly, i.e. if an inventory 
management system is used, but it is not obligatory to use these kind of systems 
 
Reliability: Most technology is reliable but there can be times when it fails.  
If the manufacturer is not in charge of the inventory, the inventory is controlled based on forecasts. With 
an original inventory there is an increased risk in stockout since the supplier himself is not observing 
the inventory (Zammori, Braglia, & Frosolini, 2009). In a Vendor Managed Inventory relationship the 
vendor pushes inventory down to the retailer in smaller batches, more frequently, based on how quickly 
the items are selling. This method distributes the inventory risk so it is shared between the buyer and 
seller. It gets the retailer as close as possible to buying exactly the amount of inventory that will sell 
(Lunka, 2015). When the entire supply chain is connected, using VMI relationships, the supply chain 
stabilizes. It reduces the risk of major disruptions that can affect all the different companies that are 
linked directly and indirectly through the supply chain (Lunka, 2015). 
When analysing the risk of transportation there are a few factors that need to be kept in mind.  Reliability, 
safety and lead time are among these factors which are considered in the selection of transportation 
companies in Section 6.1.4.2 which eliminates additional risk. 
6.6 Market Analysis 
The goal of a market analysis it to be able to gather valuable data which will help a business to know its 
customers, determine appropriate pricing and figure out the competitors’ vulnerabilities (Verdin & 
Williamson, 1997). By introducing an own-branded grease, Maersk Oil Trading Lubricants is not 
entering an unfamiliar market, since they have been a service provider on that market for many years. 
75 
==PAGE:91==
 
Analysis 
 
 
 
However what is different now is that they will be selling a product on the market which entails in more 
competitors, their former grease suppliers will now be their competitors.  
6.6.1 Competitive Advantage 
The main competitors of Maersk Oil Trading Lubricants are World Fuel Services and the current grease 
suppliers, e.g. Shell, Exxon, Chevron and Total to name a few. The competitive advantage MOTL has 
is the technical support it provides, service for individual vessels, quantity discounts since the amount 
they buy is fairly high and their close connection to their customers. A possible commercial risk by 
becoming the competitor of a supplier is loss of the supplier. As stated before the same suppliers provide 
a vessel with both the lubricating oil and grease. Ceasing the purchase of the grease from the suppliers 
is considered a  risk factor. However, the amount of grease is only a small percentage of the total amount 
bought from the suppliers so the loss would be significant for the suppliers if they were to terminate the 
lubricating oil contract with MOTL (Embleton, 2016). By introducing the Luban grease, the complexity 
on board vessels would decrease as stated in Section 6.2, which is considered value-adding to the 
customer and therefore increases their competitive advantages.  
6.7 Summary 
This chapter demonstrates the analysis conducted for the case company in question, i.e. Maersk Oil 
Trading Lubricants. The analysis was based on the quantitative and qualitative data gathered in the 
literature review and the current situation study of the company. The objective of this chapter is to cover 
the whole process of configuring the future supply chain for MOTL and narrow down options for the 
scenario building in Chapter 7.  
The chapter starts with an analysis of the case company’s product where the lubricating grease was, 
because of its steady demand and long product life cycle, categorised as a functional product. The 
appropriate supply chain strategy according to Fisher’s model is therefore to focus on efficiency in the 
supply chain, i.e. to supply the predictable demand efficiently, at the lowest cost possible.  In order to 
define the setup of each stage of the supply chain the outsourcing or insourcing of MOTL’s activities 
were considered. The conclusion was to outsource both warehousing and transportation, but since the 
manufacturer was already selected by the case company before this project it was considered out of 
scope. However, MOTL’s core competency is its service provision to Maersk Line and should be 
maintained insourced. Consequently, different warehouses and delivery companies were evaluated 
based on predefined qualitative and quantitative performance measures resulting in the selection of four 
different warehouses, A, D, E, and H, to further evaluate in scenario building in Chapter 7. Of the 
delivery companies, U and T remained for further evaluation. 
An important factor in the supply chain structure was to analyse the end-user. The fact that the end-user 
is a moving destination point was considered to be an indicator that some degree of responsiveness was 
applicable and therefore a hybrid supply chain strategy should be pursued. However, it was concluded 
that for this case company it is not relevant since the schedule of Maersk Line vessels is predefined, 
which makes them easier to locate. Consequently, the Maersk Line vessel schedule was analysed and 
harbour locations considered. In this analysis Rotterdam and Singapore are considered the most suitable 
harbours to locate warehouses and are referred to as main harbours. Vessels that arrive to these harbours 
are easy to service but the remaining vessels also require delivery and therefore the optimal delivery 
locations for these vessels were selected in order to estimate delivery cost.  
It was concluded that the appropriate business process for the case company with the functional product 
is to make it to stock. Therefore, the inventory of the lubricating grease for storage in warehouses was 
calculated. That included quantity to store, the reorder point, safety stock and frequency in product 
delivery per year from the manufacturer to warehouses. The result was that it needed to be restored 
every three months. The effect this new setup has on the case company in terms of complexity was 
discussed as well as an analysis of the possible risk factors it could entail. All information from this 
analysis is used for further analysis in the scenario building in the following chapter where the final 
recommendation and implementation are discussed for the case company. 
76 
==PAGE:92==
 
Scenarios 
 
 
 
7. Scenarios 
In this chapter the results from the analysis made in the previous chapter are used to structure seven 
different configurations of the future supply chain for Maersk Oil Trading Lubricants. As the analysis 
showed, the applicable supply chain strategy is to supply the predictable demand efficiently at the lowest 
cost possible, without reducing quality. Therefore the cost factor in each scenario is focused on, but 
advantages and disadvantages are considered simultaneously. The setup of each scenario is listed in 
Table 55 where the warehouse location is shown. It also shows whether a consignment shipment is used 
or, based on the results from the harbour location analysis in Chapter 6. Note that the consignment 
warehouse is located in Rotterdam.

The difference between scenarios is fourfold; (1) warehouse location (2) delivery responsibility (3) 
control of inventory and (4) number of vessels in- and outside Zone 1 of main harbour. As stated in 
assumptions in Section 6.2.1, the delivery cost to harbours in Zone 1 (0-100km) of the main harbour in 
question is equal to delivery cost from the warehouse to main harbour, meaning that the delivery cost 
from e.g. warehouse in Rotterdam to Rotterdam harbour is equal to the delivery cost from warehouse in 
Rotterdam to Antwerp harbour. The total cost for each scenario is calculated, including warehousing 
cost, delivery cost from manufacturer to warehouse, delivery cost from warehouse to vessel and the cost 
of purchasing the lubricating grease. The total cost is shown in two parts as the purchasing cost of grease 
is a dominant factor and therefore the change in cost between scenarios would be relatively low.  
Each scenario is illustrated in figures where the important information is gathered. An information guide 
to these figures is shown in Figure 42 which is essential for the consideration of the scenarios. In the 
following sections each scenario is presented and then the annual cost, advantages and disadvantages 
for each scenario is compared.

7.1 Scenario One - Consignment Shipment 
In scenario one the consignment shipment explained in Section 5.6 is solely used. As the information in 
Table 56 shows, the consignment shipment warehouse is Warehouse A which is located in Rotterdam. 
The grease is delivered from the manufacturer to Rotterdam by delivery company U and Group 
procurement is responsible for delivering the product from warehouse to vessel through the 
consignment. Warehouse A controls the inventory, meaning that the warehouse is responsible for 
informing MOTL when inventory levels are out of the optimal range. Grease is delivered with the 
consignment shipment to all vessels, inside and outside of Zone 1 of Rotterdam harbour. This setup 
eliminates the order process on board the vessels since the consignment shipment is delivered every 
three months, but now including the lubricating grease. As Figure 43 illustrates, the setup of scenario 
one includes the consignment shipment to 235 vessels.

and provide various options for it to choose from. Even though the consignment shipment with Group

evaluate the option if the consignment shipment would for some unknown reason no longer be available.

in the following section and a summary of each scenario is listed in Table 63. The calculations for each

7.8.1 Recommendation 
In Table 64, the seven scenarios are compared based on the estimated annual cost each setup would 
entail, including additional factors. More detailed segmentation of the cost calculations showing each 
cost factor is provided in Appendix H. The seven scenarios involve different degrees of complexity and 
control for MOTL. As Table 64  shows, each scenario is measured in complexity and control from low 
to very high. The degree of complexity is based on: (1) the number of warehouses MOTL has inventory 
in and (2) the delivery setup to vessels outside Zone 1. The control is based on the involvement of MOTL 
in the whole process. For instance, if Chemtura owns the inventory and a consignment shipment delivers 
to vessels outside Zone 1 then MOTL has low control within the supply chain. The lead time is 
established as well, where the lead time from an order is placed and delivered to a warehouse in 
Rotterdam is considered medium (30 days in production and 30 days in delivery) and long in Singapore 
(30 days in production and 60 days in transportation). In both cases the lead time is long, but medium is 
considered an appropriate representation in order to distinguish the setups.  
In Section 5.9.2, the average annual cost Maersk Line spent on grease from 2010 to 2015 is 
approximately $756.000. The comparison in Table 64 shows that each scenario is within this cost 
amount which indicates that all scenarios are viable in terms of cost. However, three scenarios are 
recommended for the case company to select from as the scenarios are not merely chosen for their annual 
cost, but for the degree of both complexity and control, the setup yields for MOTL. The recommended 
scenarios are: scenario 1, scenario 6.1 and scenario 7.2. All three scenarios are different. Scenario 6.1 
has the lowest annual cost of all the scenarios, scenario 1 is considered to entail a relatively low degree 
of complexity for MOTL and scenario 7.2 includes high control. Further discussion on each 
recommended scenario is presented in the following sections and summarised in Table 65. 
7.8.1.1 Scenario 1 – Pros and Cons 
In scenario 1 the grease is delivered to each vessel from the warehouse in Rotterdam through the 
consignment shipment, every three months. The complexity is considered relatively low since MOTL 
does not manage the delivery to vessels. MOTL is however responsible for managing delivery from the 
manufacturer to the warehouse in Rotterdam, where the warehouse manages the inventory in close 
contact with MOTL. This scenario is therefore considered yielding low/medium complexity and 
medium control due to the inventory. It should be noted that the consignment shipment is shared with 
other partners within Maersk Group that also require delivery of necessary equipment used on board the 
vessels, such as spare parts for machinery. For this reason the cost of delivery is shared, hence the 
relatively low price. This scenario is also recommended for the following reasons:  
 
The consignment shipment is considered a relatively safe option. It is a familiar process that 
was implemented more than a decade ago and each shipment has many stakeholders, which 
implies a carefully managed process. 
 
The warehouse is located in Rotterdam which has shorter order lead time than in Singapore. It 
is considered that inventory management is simplified with lower lead times since it is easier to 
respond to sudden changes and manage inventory level. 
 
The consignment reduces complexity in delivery for MOTL 
 
The annual cost is relatively low considering the simplification of delivery to all vessels. 
However, this setup of the supply chain relies heavily on the consignment shipment so the possibility of 
the shipment to stop for some reason should be considered. The consignment shipment yields less 
flexibility in delivery, since the shipment has a predefined delivery time every three months. 
7.8.1.2 Scenario 6.1 – Pros and Cons 
In scenario 6.1, Chemtura and the consignment shipment are the key functions in the supply chain. 
Chemtura owns inventory until the product leaves its warehouses, meaning that MOTL is not involved 
in managing the inventory. Chemtura delivers the product from the production site to warehouses, then 
from warehouse to agent at the harbour, for vessels inside Zone 1 in both Rotterdam and Singapore. As 
Table 63 shows, the number of remaining vessels is 65. MOTL is responsible for delivery to warehouse 
86 
==PAGE:102==
 
Scenarios 
 
 
 
in Rotterdam and consignment shipment is used to deliver to these 65 vessels outside Zone 1.This 
scenario is further recommended because:  
 
The scenario involves the lowest cost of all scenarios  
 
The setup is measured to entail low complexity for MOTL since the inventory managed for the 
consignment shipment is merely for 30% of the vessel fleet and MOTL is not responsible for 
delivery of product to vessels. 
 
Chemtura owns a great deal of the inventory, therefore less capital tied up in inventory.  
However, it should be noted that MOTLs position in this setup is weakened since the manufacturer 
manages a great deal of the supply chain and is close to the customer, which is always dangerous if the 
company does not want to be cut out of the equation. This is however considered implausible since the 
manufacturer merely talks to the agent at the harbour, but never actually to the customer Nonetheless 
MOTL should be aware of this risk factor. 
7.8.1.3 Scenario 7.2 – Pros and Cons 
If Table 64 is considered, Scenario 3.2., Scenario 5.2 and Scenario 7.2 are relatively similar but the 
warehouse location varies. In all these scenarios MOTL is responsible for managing the delivery to 
vessels outside of Zone 1 from warehouse location, with delivery company U, and from the warehouse 
to the vessels inside Zone 1 These scenarios give MOTL more control since the warehouse manages 
inventory in close contact with MOTL, i.e. consignment is not considered nor Chemtura’s warehouses.  
Of these three scenarios, Scenario 3.2 entails the lowest annual cost. However, if Table 63 is considered 
for that scenario, the number of vessels outside Zone 1 are 173 which indicates a very high complexity 
for MOTL because of frequent deliveries to vessels. In Scenario 5.2 the annual cost is high, warehouse 
location is merely in Singapore, therefore long lead time, but complexity is considered lower than in 
3.2. due to a lower number of vessels outside Zone 1. However, scenario 7.2. is considered the best 
option of these three similar scenarios. The reason is because even though the cost is higher than in 
scenario 3.2, the fact that the warehouses are located in two different continents provides more flexibility 
in inventory and delivery, since now there are two sources that provide products, if something is perhaps 
miscalculated. This scenario is further recommended because of these reasons:  
 
High control for MOTL of the whole supply chain since there is no other key actor 
 
Locating warehouses in two continents enables increased possibility in managing the delivery 
to vessels for two main reasons: since there are many vessels that arrive within Zone 1 of both 
harbours and also if there is a miscalculation the vessel has two sources to receive a product 
from. 
 
The increased complexity through many functions enables the company to expand its customer 
segment. It is considered easier to expand with this setup than with, for instance, the 
consignment shipment since it is merely for vessels owned by Maersk Group. 
However, this scenario results in a higher cost than the prior suggestions. The warehousing and delivery 
costs are more than three times higher than in the other two scenarios. This is nevertheless within the 
estimated spending cost mentioned in Section 5.9.2, which is approximately $340.000. If the company 
intends to expand this setup should be considered, as the possibility of negotiating lower prices is 
expectable. Moreover, this setup includes many new processes for MOTL and therefore the risk of errors 
increases, which should be noted while assessing this scenario.  
7.8.1.4 Summary of recommendations 
A summary of advantages and disadvantages for the three recommendations is provided in Table 65. 
Additionally, the table shows the possibility for MOTL to reduce the sales price of the new lubricating 
grease for their customer. The average annual grease cost for Maersk Line is $755.086, as earlier 
mentioned, including warehousing and delivery. The grease cost of that average annual cost was 
however $417.740, leaving approximately $340.000 for delivery and warehousing. The difference 
between the average annual cost Maersk Line has spent in the last five years and the annual cost each 
scenario entails, provides the possibility or the room MOTL has to decide on the sales price it wants to 
offer to Maersk Line. In other words, a higher difference provides the higher possibility of lower sale 
87 
==PAGE:103==

price. As Table 64 demonstrates, the scenario that provides the lowest sales price possibility is scenario 
6.1. This is an additional factor for Maersk Oil Trading Lubricant to consider when deciding on a 
scenario to proceed with. An additional factor that was however not calculated is the set up cost of these 
suggested supply chains. It is however concluded that the company has to implement the chosen 
recommendation step by step, meaning that it will not start providing all vessels of Maersk Line at once 
and therefore an additional setup cost was not considered relevant for the project.

7.8.2  Implementation 
By changing its current supply chain to the suggested future supply chain, MOTL will be making a 
change to its processes. When making an operational change the Proski ADKAR model (Hiatt, 2006) 
could be used. The ADKAR model is a goal-oriented change management model to guide individual 
and organisational change (Hiatt, 2006). This model is chosen as an example of a change model since it 
is relatively simple and illustrates both the different phases of a change project and the people side of 
the change (see Figure 50). The general phases of a change project are: business need, concept & design, 
implementation and post implementation. A decision icon is located in Figure 50 where MOTL is placed 
between concept & design and implementation. Prior to this case study MOTL identified a business 
opportunity, i.e. purchase grease directly from manufacturer and eliminate the “middleman”. This case 
study includes the concept & design of the project with three different recommendations listed in section 
7.8.1.4 as the possible business solution for the company. Now the company has to make a decision 
based on the recommended scenarios and move into the implementation phase of the model.   
 
 
 
Figure 50: ADKAR model for change management (Hiatt, 2006). 
 
88 
==PAGE:104==
 
Scenarios 
 
 
 
The SCOR process reference model was introduced in Section 2.9. The model includes a standard 
description of management processes and metrics to measure process performance (Supply Chain 
Council, 2010). These processes could be applied in the implementation stage along with performance 
measurements of the processes for the post-implementation. As described with the SCOR model, the 
processes within the framework help standardise the description of the supply chain architecture and the 
implementation of it to the company (Supply Chain Council, 2010). With this study an architecture of a 
supply chain has been proposed but a standardisation for the processes in the chosen recommendation 
have to be made. The implementation of the architecture is therefore the next step, where a combination 
of the change model and the SCOR framework is recommended for the implementation of the suggested 
future supply chain at MOTL.  
According to Hiatt (2006) the most commonly cited reason for project failure is problems with the 
people side of change. The future supply chain will affect various actors within the current supply chain, 
for instance by changing the supply chain the procurement process changes. The operational department, 
previously mentioned in Section 4.1.1 make purchase orders for the relevant grease for each vessel, now 
the supplier has the product on stock and manages the delivery to the agent at each harbour. With the 
suggested future supply chain the job requirement for the operational team could also involve 
communication with a delivery company, manufacturer and a warehouse, depending on the 
standardisation of the process and how tasks will be divided within the department.  The people side of 
change in the ADKAR model are described in Table 66 where each step in the individual’s journey for 
a successful change is described (Hiatt, 2006). 
 
Table 66: Five milestones individuals must achieve for a successful change (Hiatt, 2006) 
Milestones 
Clarification  
Awareness  
Be aware of the need for change 
Desire 
Have a desire to participate and support the change 
Knowledge 
Know how to change and what the change looks like 
Ability 
Have the ability to implement the change on an everyday basis 
Reinforcement 
Have a goal to keep the change in place 
 
The three recommended scenarios have different fundamentals and stakeholders. In Section 4.3 a 
stakeholder analysis was conducted to get an overview of each stakeholder in the study. The main 
stakeholders for each recommended scenario are the same, i.e. customer, senior executive, steering 
group and employees. However the suppliers differ within each scenario for example the storage facility.

by the change and should therefore be kept in mind when making an implementation plan. In

7.9 Summary 
In this chapter the results from the analysis made in Chapter 6 were used to structure seven different 
configurations of the supply chain for Maersk Oil Trading Lubricants. The aim was to evaluate the 
different possibilities for the case company and provide it with various options to choose from.  
Each scenario was structured on the base of transportation from the manufacturer in Canada to a 
warehouse in Rotterdam, Singapore or both Rotterdam and Singapore. The transportation between the 
warehouses to the vessels was divided into two parts; (1) delivery to vessels arriving within 100 km of 
the harbour location (Zone 1) and (2) delivery to vessels arriving outside 100 km of the harbour location. 
Consequently, the inventory held on stock in the warehouses is variable in each scenario, dependent on 
the number of vessels each warehouse services. The consignment shipment introduced in Section 5.6 
was used in some scenarios, mostly for delivery to vessels outside the 100 km of the harbour. 
Additionally, there were two options of warehousing, either Vendor Managed Inventory where the 
manufacturer (Chemtura) owns the product and keeps it on stock in its own warehouse in either 
91 
==PAGE:107==
 
Scenarios 
 
 
 
Rotterdam or Singapore (or both) or the other option where the inventory is owned by MOTL and 
managed by the warehouse manager in close contact with MOTL. In these types of scenarios MOTL is 
more involved in the whole process. 
The annual cost for all scenarios was compared, as well as the complexity of the supply chain for MOTL, 
control and the lead time, that is the time it takes to manufacturer and deliver a product to the warehouse. 
From these factors three scenarios are suggested for the case company to choose from, i.e. Scenario 1, 
Scenario 6.1 and Scenario 7.2. The suggested scenarios are dependent on what the case company aims 
for, that is: low risk, low cost or high control. The chapter ends with a discussion of necessary 
consideration on implementing each scenario into Maersk Oil Trading Lubricant operation.  
 
  
92 
==PAGE:108==
 
Discussion 
 
 
 
8. Discussion 
In this chapter the entire report is discussed. The chapter is divided into theoretical discussions and 
practical implication. The theoretical discussions involve the literature part of the report, the research 
gap and the contribution to theory this case study entails. The practical implication focuses on the case 
company, the work performed and the contribution this case study entails both for the case company 
and other companies in similar industries. Finally, the limitation of the study is presented.  
8.1 Theoretical Discussions 
In Chapter 1 in this report a research gap was identified. The literature shows a limited amount of 
research on both supply chain management in the maritime industry and the effect an end user of a 
product has on the supply chain strategy selection. Therefore a research question was formulated with 
the objective to answer the research question: “What is the relevant supply chain strategy for a product 
in the maritime industry with a moving destination point”. In order to obtain answers, a case study was 
carried out on a company within the maritime industry. The study was based on the existing knowledge 
in literature that was considered applicable for supply chain management in the maritime industry. 
However, the limited amount of literature in the maritime industry resulted in a knowledge gap, 
regarding the effect the end user has on the supply chain. 
Fisher’s framework for selecting supply chain strategies was used as a guideline in the case study. The 
model states that if a product is functional, the relevant supply chain strategy is to focus on efficiency, 
to supply the predictable demand efficiently at the lowest cost possible, without reducing quality.  
The case study entailed a functional product with a steady demand, which indicated that an efficient 
supply chain was applicable for the case company, which answers first two of the hypothesis made in 
Chapter 1. However, a hypothesis was made, stating that even though the demand is steady, the unsteady 
destination point could entail some degree of responsiveness, perhaps in high inventory levels, because 
it is difficult to locate where and when a vessel requires a delivery. Nevertheless, the literature states 
efficiency aims for low inventory levels since high inventory can entail high cost, while responsive 
supply chain is not focused on cost but more on the availability of the product. This hypothesis is 
therefore on the contrary to Fisher’s theory. However, in the literature there are empirical studies on 
hybrid strategies that are proven to work but they are better known strategies for innovative products, 
where the process is efficient until the decoupling point and responsive downstream. This was 
nonetheless considered an indication to further analyse the impact the end user has on the decision of a 
supply chain strategy.   
The analysis of the end user of the case study resulted in a rejection of the hypothesis for that case 
company. This was due to the fact that the company’s vessels have a predefined schedule. It is 
nonetheless considered a possibility that a hybrid strategy is applicable if the destination point is moving. 
For further exploration on this subject, a framework is proposed. 
8.1.1 The Framework 
It is believed that supply chain strategies in the maritime industry are dependent on the product and the 
end user. Therefore, the proposed framework represents two factors to consider when deciding on the 
supply chain strategy. These two factors are the behaviour of both the end user and the product: how 
does the end user reach the product and vice versa. In other words, if the relationship between the product 
and the end user is identified, the relevant strategy can be found by using this framework. The structure 
of the framework is based on the analysis made in Section 6.1.5. It is presented as a decision-making 
matrix, where each quadrant represents different relationships between the product and the end user, as 
illustrated in Figure 51. As explained in Section 6.1.5, a fixed location means that the location of the 
product or end user is known and the moving location that the location is unknown.  
 
93 
==PAGE:109==
 
Discussion 
 
 
 
The case company under study in this report, had a predefined vessel schedule with fixed routes. 
Furthermore, the product was either delivered to the vessels (where the vessel had a fixed location and 
the product was moving towards the vessel) or the vessel arrived in the harbour where the warehouse 
was located (the product had a fixed location and the vessel moved towards it). An efficient strategy 
was considered applicable in both cases, since the demand remains steady. However, it is proposed that 
if the product is a moving unit and the end user does not have a fixed location, a hybrid strategy is 
applicable. The scenario where both product and end user are fixed units, indicates that the product is 
not tangible. This quadrant is considered for an internet service of some kind used on board the vessels, 
where the customer does not require tailing the product (the service) and the product does not tail the 
customer. It is matched with a responsive strategy since internet services entail high responsiveness. 
This quadrant was however not considered in the conducted study, where the other three were identified. 
It should therefore be noted that this quadrant is on an earlier development stage than the other three 
and requires further investigation. A summary of the quadrants is depicted in Table 69. 
 
 
 
Figure 51: The decision-making framework for supply chains in the maritime industry.  
 
Table 69: Information for each quadrant 
Quadrant 
Applicable Strategy 
Clarification

8.1.2 The Six Step Guideline 
In addition to the framework, a guideline is provided for selecting the relevant strategy for supply chains 
in the maritime industry. That is, the stages that should be considered when choosing the relevant supply 
chain strategy, as Figure 52 depicts. The six stages that are proposed in the guideline are listed as 
follows:  
 
Identify: This step addresses the identification of both the end user and the product: who is the 
end user and what type is the product. 
 
Analyse: This step covers the analysis of the behaviour of the end user and the relationship 
between the product and the end user. Here is where the proposed framework should be used.  
 
Evaluate & Decide: Henceforth, the results from the analysis are evaluated and the decision is 
made on whether the strategy from the matrix is a strategy that should be focused on 
 
Configure: In this step the supply chain is configured, where each stage is evaluated and aligned 
with the objectives of the selected strategy 
 
Implement: The processes the configured supply chain strategy entails are implemented to the 
organisation. 
 
Monitor: Each process is monitored and measured to establish continuous improvement.  
 
 
 
 
Figure 52: The six step guideline for supply chain strategy decisions in the maritime industry. 
8.1.3 Further Work on the Framework 
The research question of this project was answered for the case company under study, i.e. the relevant 
supply chain strategy is efficiency. However, space remains for further investigation since the end user 
in the case company had a predefined vessel schedule with fixed routes. Therefore, the next step should 
be to investigate a supply chain in the maritime industry with a moving destination point that does not 
contain a predefined vessel schedule and see if the results support the proposed framework. The 
proposed framework is not based on existing frameworks so therefore it is noted that for it to be practical 
there is need for further investigations.

the Maritime

Discussion 
 
 
 
8.2 Practical Implications 
Prior to this study Maersk Oil Trading Lubricants had identified the business opportunity to buy grease 
directly from the manufacturer and supply its own brand of lubricating grease.  The manufacturer had 
been chosen and the yearly product demand for each grease type was provided along with the customer 
for the case analysis, i.e. Maersk Line. This business opportunity required an architecture of a new 
supply chain which MOTL had no experience in. The companies’ expectation at the starting point of 
this study was to know if the project would be applicable for their business, how the supply chain could 
be setup and if the changes would entail lower cost.    
The study resulted in three recommended options based on advantages and disadvantages of 13 different 
proposed scenarios. A configuration of a future supply chain was illustrated for each scenario and 
evaluated based on complexity, cost and control. MOTL now has to make a decision which of the 
recommended options suits the business and future goals. If the implementation of the chosen 
recommendation results in a success, this project can be used as a starting point to expand further to the 
fleet within Maersk Tankers and Maersk supply service. Henceforth, MOTL can strengthen their 
financial situation and expand their operation from being a service provider dependant on Maersk Group 
and become a merchandiser with increased cash flow since now it would own the product which opens 
the possibility of third party customers.  
The case study is considered further useful for companies which have the same speculations as MOTL, 
regarding the strategy and consequently how to structure the supply chain. It is considered that 
companies within the maritime industry and other industries with a moving destination point, can benefit 
from using the six step guideline presented in the previous section and use it as a starting point for a 
supply chain strategy decision. In Table 70 the guidelines are demonstrated with the use of Maersk Line 
vessel fleet as an end user. The study for the case company merely included the first four steps in the 
guideline, i.e. the last two steps: implementation and monitor, are out of scope.  
 
 
Table 70: The six step guideline for supply chain strategy decisions in the maritime industry 
Identify 
End user: Maersk Line vessel fleet 
Product: Lubricating grease 
Analyse 
The product is first analysed, i.e. if it is a functional or an innovative product. If the 
product is functional the decision making framework for supply chains could be 
used to analyse if the supply chain strategy should be efficient, responsive or 
hybrid.  For Maersk Line, both the product and the vessels have fixed and moving 
locations which results in an efficient strategy

==PAGE:112==
 
Discussion 
 
 
 
8.3 Limitation of the Study 
The empirical study conducted in this report had its limitations. The limited amount in literature 
regarding supply chain management in the maritime specifically and on the end user of a product, made 
the process less structured than if clear guidelines were available.  
Due to time constraints the project was narrowed down and could not be carried out in the same 
multitude as was initially hoped. This is referred to the analysis of the other vessel fleet within Maersk 
Group, which are Maersk Tankers and Supply Service. This is something that could have provided more 
results in regards to end user since both of these vessel fleets do not have a predefined schedule.  
The assumptions that were made for the harbour location analysis is also considered a limitation. Two 
main warehouse locations were chosen to narrow down the magnitude of that part of the analysis. For 
this reason it is possible that other warehouse locations could have been better options.  
Furthermore, from the assumed applicable warehouse location the vessel schedule was analysed by 
using excel as the analysis tool which possibly did not provide the optimal solution. An Operations 
Research model would be necessary to use for this to be optimised which was not made, due to time 
constraints. The OR model could then optimise the delivery network, with the objective to minimise 
cost and could then return the optimal warehouse locations as well.  
97 
==PAGE:113==
 
Conclusion 
 
 
 
9. Conclusion 
The project started out with identifying the research gap in literature and from that a research question 
was developed in order to address it. The gap indicated lack of research on: (1) the end user of a supply 
chain and the effect it has on supply chain strategy decisions, and (2) the connection between supply 
chain management and the maritime industry. Consequently the research question “What is the relevant 
supply chain strategy for a product in the maritime industry with a moving destination point” was 
formulated. 
Researchers such as Milogram & Roberts (1990), de Groote (1994) and Fisher (1997) identified that 
aligning supply chain strategies and the nature of products is essential to achieve long-term competitive 
advantage. This identification was used throughout the study, in particular Fisher’s Product-Supply 
Chain Matrix,which was made to help managers on the selection of a supply chain strategy. According 
to the matrix, functional products are better suited for an efficient supply chain, while innovative 
products call for a more responsive one, which can be a useful knowledge in many industries. Since then 
many researchers have conducted studies in order to test the matrix, some supporting it and others not. 
Some of these studies indicated an existence of hybrid strategies where both efficiency and 
responsiveness are used in the same supply chain. This was considered worth investigating to see if it 
was applicable in the maritime industry. Consequently, hypotheses were made that products used on 
board vessels are functional products and therefore an efficient supply chain strategy is relevant. 
However, it was also hypothesised that since the destination point of the product is moving the supply 
chain has to be responsive and therefore some degree of a hybrid strategy is applicable.   
In order to address the identified research gap and see if Fisher’s matrix is applicable in the maritime 
industry where the end user is moving, an empirical case study was made. Maersk Oil Trading was the 
case company for this study since it fulfilled the criteria for the research question, i.e. the company is 
within the maritime industry and it was considering changing its supply chain strategy. 
The case companies was analysed on the base of their current situation by using the available literature 
that was considered applicable for this study. A future supply chain was suggested for the company 
where three different scenarios were recommended. The analysis indicated that the product the company 
was supplying the vessels with (lubricating grease), is a functional product and therefore an efficient 
supply chain strategy is applicable. The end user was however analysed in order to see if it would change 
the strategy of having an efficient supply chain, perhaps in increased inventory of the grease to respond 
to the variable location of the vessels. The end user in the case study was the vessel fleet of Maersk 
Line. The analysis concluded that even though the end user was moving it did not change the decision 
on focusing on efficiency in the supply chain. However, this was due to the fact that Maersk Line’s 
vessel’s schedule is predefined, which makes their location steady. This indicates that there is still room 
for further investigation on vessels that do not have predefined schedules. Maersk Oil Trading intends 
to expand the project made in this thesis into other fleets within Maersk that do not have a predefined 
schedule. This would be interesting to use for further investigation on the impact the vessels have on the 
selection of a supply chain strategy.  
From this project a framework was proposed. The framework was intended for companies within the 
maritime industry demonstrating that if the relationship between a product and the end user is known, 
the relevant strategy can be obtained from the framework. Additionally, a six step guidelines were made 
in order for other companies in similar industries and with the same objectives as the case study to 
benefit from.  
In conclusion, it was possible to find the relevant supply chain strategy for a product in the maritime 
industry with a moving destination point but it is also concluded that there is need for further 
investigation on the subject.

Appendix B 
 
 
 
Appendix B 
 
Table 71: International Commercial Terms or 'Incoterms' (Inbound Logistics, 2003) 
Incoterm 
Clarificati
on 
Characteristics 
DEPARTURE  
EXW 
Ex works 
 Seller makes goods available to be collected at their premises  
 Buyer is responsible for all other risks, transportation cost, duties 
and insurance. 
MAIN CARRIAGE NOT PAID BY SELLER 
FAS 
Free 
Alongside 
Ship 
 Seller transports the goods alongside the vessel at the specified 
port of export. There, risk of loss shifts to the buyer.  
 Buyer is responsible for the cost and risk involved in loading 
them.  
FCA 
Free 
Carrier 
 Seller clears the goods for export, to the buyer’s carrier at a 
specified place.  
 Buyer is responsible for risk of loss from that point and all costs 
associated with the transport to final destination. 
FOB 
Free On 
Board 
 Seller delivers goods for export and loads them onto a specified 
vessel, and clears customs in the country of export.  
 When goods are aboard, the risk of loss falls on the buyer, who 
pays all cost from that point on. 
MAIN CARRIAGE PAID BY SELLER 
CFR 
Cost and 
Freight 
 Seller is responsible for clearing the goods for export and pays the 
costs to bring the goods to the port.  
 Buyer is responsible for risk of loss once the goods are aboard the 
vessel.  
 Insurance, unloading goods, clear customs and transport to 
destination is paid by the buyer. 
CIF 
Cost, 
Insurance 
& Freight 
 Seller is responsible for delivery of goods aboard the vessel and 
clearing customs in the country of export.  
 Seller pays for insurance.  
 Buyer is responsible for risks when the goods are loaded onto the 
vessel.  
 Buyer clears customs in the country of import and pays for all 
other transport and insurance in the country import 
CPT 
Carriage 
Paid To 
 Seller pays to transport the goods to the specified destination.  
 Responsibility for the goods transfers to the buyer when the seller 
passes them to the first carrier. 
CIP 
Carriage 
and 
Insurance 
Paid 
 Seller pays for insurance as well as transport to the specified 
destination.  
 Responsibility for the goods transfers to the buyer when the seller 
passes them to the first carrier 
 
 
ARRIVAL 
DAF 
Delivered 
At Frontier 
 Seller pays for transport to a specified frontier at an agreed 
destination.

==PAGE:122==
 
Appendix B 
 
 
 
 Buyer takes responsibility once the goods are unloaded at the 
frontier.  
 Buyer is responsible for the cost of importing the goods.  
DES 
Delivered 
Ex-Ship 
 Buyer is responsible for all costs to deliver the goods to a 
specified port of destination.  
 Goods are made available for the buyer aboard the vessel. 
 Seller is responsible for all costs and risk of loss prior to unloading 
at the port of destination. 
DEQ 
Delivered 
Ex-Quay 
 Seller is responsible for all costs in transporting the goods to the 
quay at the port of destination.  
 Buyer pays duties, clear customs, and bears all cost and risk of 
loss from that point on. 
DDU 
Delivered 
Duty 
Unpaid 
 Seller pays for transport to the specified destination, but the buyer 
pays the cost of importing the goods.  
 Seller takes responsibility for the goods until they’re ready to be 
unloaded by the buyer.  
DDP 
Delivered 
Duty Paid 
 Seller is responsible for delivering the goods to the specified 
destination in the buyer’s country, including all costs involved.  
 Seller provides door-to-door delivery, including customs 
clearance in both port of export and import.  
 Seller bears risk of loss until goods are delivered at the buyers 
premises.

Appendix G 
 
 
 
Appendix G 
This appendix is in the excel-file named HarbourLocations on the USB stick enclosed. Sheets Scenario 
A1, Scenario A2, Scenario B1, Scenario B2 and Scenario C show which harbour is allocated to each 
vessel within Maersk Line. The columns represent the vessels and the selected harbours are highlighted 
with a yellow colour, leaving one yellow harbour in each column. Sheets Cost Sc_A, Cost Sc_B and 
Cost Sc_C show calculations on delivery cost for scenario A, B and C.

Appendix J 
 
 
 
Appendix J 
Performance measurements with SCOR framework 
The SCOR model is an approach for companies to achieve outstanding performance. It is a management 
tool which is used to address, improve and communicate supply chain management decisions. SCOR 
developed a set of metrics to measure supply chains in five perspectives; reliability, responsiveness, 
flexibility, cost and assets. The business activities associated with all phases to satisfy customer demand 
are described in the SCOR Model the procedure for measuring the performance of a supply chain is 
illustrated in Figure 53. 
 
 
Figure 53: The steps of supply chain performance analysis. 
Choose performance metrics 
The metrics are chosen in the first step. According to the Supply Chain Council (2010). Companies 
should at least choose one performance metric from each perspective. All the different metrics are shown 
in the SCOR Model below. The metric were chosen based on a meeting with three employees at MOTL 
(Head of MOTL, daily manager and a lubricant specialist) and are based on the case companies’ 
objectives for their supply chain. . The chosen metrics are shown in Table 88. Many of the metrics in 
appendix H are relevant for the new supply chain, e.g. the manufacturer, warehouses etc. but not 
necessarily MOTL. Therefore, the company relies on outstanding performance from their suppliers in 
order to fulfil their customers demand.  
Table 88: Chosen performance metrics. 
Perspective 
Metric 
Reliability 
Documentation accuracy 
Responsiveness 
Cycle time for responding to customers’ requirements 
Agility 
Adaptability  
Cost 
Transportation cost 
Cost 
Warehousing cost 
Assets 
Inventory 
 
Define performance metrics 
In step two the performance metrics are defined, a definition of the metrics are shown in Table 89. In 
order for the employees in the case company to understand the chosen metrics and know what is 
expected of them it is optimal to have a clear definition.

transportation & maritime, in order to evaluate the characteristics to set up the most appropriate index 
for the targets. An example of targets made by these companies is listed in Table 90.

Evaluation and Improving 
For each perspective a SCOR card can be made as the example in Table 91 demonstrates. In step four 
the SCOR cards in all perspectives are listed and are shown in below in Table 91. With a SCOR card a 
company can identify its target and easily understand the gap between the actual value and the target 
value of a specific metric. It also helps companies to manage performance based on criteria. After a 
SCOR card has been developed the company has to decide a threshold value of the gap rate. The 
indicators that exceed the threshold are considered to have a poor performance. If the index has a higher 
gap rate than the threshold value of the gap, the index needs to be adjusted and improved. (Chen, Du, 
Hu, Lin, & Li, 2005) 
Table 91: Examples of SCOR cards in the responsiveness perspective.
